SPINE
61.
Spinal Stenosis (Lumbar — Conservative)
Phase 1 — Symptom Management & Flexion-Based Mobility (Week 0–6)
n GOALS
• Reduce neurogenic claudication (buttock and leg
pain with walking)
• Identify directional preference — lumbar flexion
typically relieves symptoms
• Patient education: stenosis is manageable; surgery
not always needed
• Improve walking tolerance via symptom-relief
positions
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid lumbar extension — narrows spinal canal
further
• Monitor for progressive neurological deficit
(weakness, bowel/bladder) — surgical referral
• Walking aids (walker, shopping trolley lean) reduce
symptoms — use as needed
• Cauda equina symptoms = emergency referral
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Posterior Pelvic Tilt (supine) 3 × 20 reps, 5 sec hold Flatten lumbar lordosis — opens spinal
canal; core activation without extension
Knee-to-Chest Stretch (bilateral) 3 × 45 sec Lumbar flexion — opens posterior canal;
immediate symptom relief position
Seated Lumbar Flexion (slumped) 3 × 20 reps Canal opening position — useful rest
position for symptom relief during walking
Stationary Bike (slightly forward lean) 20–30 min Flexion-biased cardiovascular conditioning
— reduces claudication vs treadmill
Aquatic Walking (pool) 20–30 min Offloaded walking — allows greater
distance before symptom onset
Abdominal Isometrics (supine) 3 × 15 reps, 10 sec hold Transversus abdominis — stabilise spine in
flexion-neutral position
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Walking tolerance improving (>10 min without stopping)
• Pain £4/10 with daily activities
• Understanding of symptom-relief positions
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 10
Phase 2 — Strengthening & Walking Tolerance (Week 6–16)
n GOALS
• Core and lower limb strength to support spinal canal
dynamics
• Increase walking tolerance progressively
• Improve quality of life and functional independence
• Reduce fear-avoidance of movement
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid lumbar extension exercises (prone press-up,
hyperextension)
• Progress walking gradually — use symptom-relief
rest breaks
• Monitor for fall risk — foot drop, proprioceptive
deficits common
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Dead Bug (neutral pelvis) 3 × 10 reps Transversus abdominis + multifidus —
maintain posterior pelvic tilt throughout
Side-lying Hip Abduction 3 × 20 reps Glute medius — reduces lateral trunk shift
and claudication pattern
Bridge (progression to single-leg) 3 × 15 reps Posterior chain — glute max activation in
neutral/flexion bias
Sit-to-Stand Progression 3 × 15 reps Functional quad and glute strengthening —
critical for independence
Treadmill Walking (slight incline forward lean) 15–30 min Reduce lumbar extension moment —
increases walking tolerance vs flat treadmill
Leg Press (bilateral, 60–90°) 3 × 15 reps Lower limb strength without spinal
extension loading
Cycling (recumbent or upright lean forward) 30 min Sustained flexion-biased cardio — build
tolerance progressively
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Walking ³20 min with adequate rest breaks
• Sit-to-stand independent without upper limb assist
• Lower limb strength ³75% symmetry
Phase 3 — Functional Independence & Long-Term Management (Week 16+)
n GOALS
• Maximum walking tolerance for age/fitness level
• Community ambulation independence
• Long-term self-management and lifestyle
modification
n PRECAUTIONS
• Surgical decompression (laminectomy) if inadequate
response at 6 months
• Weight management reduces spinal canal
compression forces
• Fall prevention program if proprioceptive or motor
deficits present
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 11
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Nordic Walking (poles — forward lean) Progressive distance Poles promote lumbar flexion —
significantly increases walking tolerance in
stenosis
Pool Walking / Swimming (non-freestyle) 30–45 min Sustained pain-free cardiovascular loading
— lifelong recommendation
Progressive Core Endurance Program 3 × week Maintain canal-opening muscle support —
plank, dead bug, bridge
Balance Training (single-leg, foam) 3 × 45 sec each Fall prevention — common comorbidity in
lumbar stenosis
Community Walking Program Daily, graded 10% weekly increase — prioritise
consistency over intensity
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Community ambulation ³30 min with manageable symptoms
• Quality of life scores improving (ODI, ZCQ)
• Self-managed home program established
REFERENCES & EVIDENCE BASE
• Weinstein JN et al. (2008). Surgical vs nonsurgical therapy for lumbar spinal stenosis. NEJM.
• Ammendolia C et al. (2013). Nonoperative treatment for lumbar spinal stenosis with neurogenic claudication. Cochrane.
• Delitto A et al. (2015). Low back disorders. JOSPT Clinical Practice Guidelines.
• Fritz JM et al. (2007). Lumbar spinal stenosis: a review of current concepts in evaluation, management, and outcome
measurements. Arch Phys Med Rehabil.

62.
Facet Joint Dysfunction (Lumbar)
Phase 1 — Pain Control & Mobilisation (Week 0–3)
n GOALS
• Reduce lumbar facet joint pain (typically worse with
extension, rotation)
• Restore segmental lumbar mobility
• Identify directional preference and pain-relieving
positions
• Patient education — highly treatable with
conservative management
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid end-range lumbar extension and ipsilateral
rotation in acute phase
• No high-velocity manipulation in acute severe
presentation
• Rule out fracture, malignancy, infection (red flags)
• Facet pain is often worse in morning — activity
warms up joint
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Knee-to-Chest Stretches (single + bilateral) 3 × 45 sec Flexion unloads facet joint — primary
pain-relief position
Lumbar Rotation Stretch (supine hook-lying) 3 × 30 sec each Gentle facet mobilisation — stop at pain
barrier, do not force
Cat-Camel 3 × 10 reps Segmental lumbar flexion/extension mobility
— improve synovial fluid circulation
Posterior Pelvic Tilt (standing, wall) 3 × 20 reps Reduce lumbar lordosis — offload posterior
facets
Walking (paced) 15–20 min × 2/day Controlled loading — better than rest; use
pain as guide
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Pain £3/10 with daily activities
• Lumbar ROM improving — especially flexion
• Morning stiffness under 30 min
Phase 2 — Stabilisation & Strengthening (Week 3–10)
n GOALS
• Lumbar and hip strength to reduce facet joint load
• Restore full lumbar ROM
• Return to full ADLs and work
• Motor control in functional tasks
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid heavy extension-loaded exercises
(hyperextensions, good mornings)
• Progress loading gradually — pain flares expected
and manageable
• Correct hip hinge pattern to reduce lumbar facet
compression
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 13
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Dead Bug (full progression) 3 × 10 reps each Deep stabiliser activation — maintain
posterior pelvic tilt throughout
Bird Dog 3 × 10 reps each Multifidus + contralateral glute — facet joint
dynamic stabilisation
Side Plank 3 × 30–45 sec each Lateral core — reduce lateral shear on facet
joints
Hip Flexor Stretch (kneeling) 3 × 45 sec each Tight psoas increases lumbar lordosis and
facet compression
Glute Strengthening (bridge, hip thrust) 3 × 15 reps Posterior pelvic tilt via glute max — reduces
facet joint load
Lumbar Rotation Mobility (standing) 3 × 15 reps each Progressive facet joint loading in rotation as
pain allows
Walking (progressive duration) 30 min daily Graded loading — 10% weekly increase
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Pain £1/10 with all ADLs
• Full lumbar ROM — especially extension improving
• Return to full work duties
Phase 3 — Return to Full Activity (Week 10–20)
n GOALS
• Return to sport and manual labour
• Load tolerance for bending, lifting, twisting
• Long-term recurrence prevention
n PRECAUTIONS
• Facet joint injections if conservative management
fails at 8–12 weeks
• Maintain core program long-term — recurrence
common without exercise
• 10% weekly loading increase rule
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Romanian Deadlift 4 × 10 reps Hip hinge — protect lumbar facets via
posterior chain loading
Goblet Squat 4 × 12 reps Full squat — progress load as facet
tolerance improves
Pallof Press 3 × 12 reps each Anti-rotation — protect facets from
rotational overload in sport
Sport-Specific Training Progressive sessions Gradual return with technique coaching —
avoid end-range extension under load
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Pain-free with all sport and work demands
• Independent maintenance program
• No recurrence for 4 weeks

BMC Musculoskelet Disord.

Anesthesiology.

63.
Sacroiliac Joint Dysfunction
Phase 1 — Pain Control & Joint Stabilisation (Week 0–4)
n GOALS
• Reduce SIJ pain (typically posterior iliac, buttock,
may refer to posterior thigh)
• SIJ compression via sacral belt — reduce shear
forces
• Identify provocative positions and movements (often
unilateral loading)
• Patient education — SIJ contributes to 15–30% of
LBP
n PRECAUTIONS
• Confirm SIJ diagnosis — cluster of 3+ positive
provocation tests (Thigh Thrust, FABER, Gaenslen)
• Rule out inflammatory causes (ankylosing
spondylitis, psoriatic arthritis)
• Avoid single-leg stance loading in acute phase —
maximises SIJ shear
• SIJ belt worn 24h initially — including sleeping
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Posterior Pelvic Tilt (supine) 3 × 20 reps Transversus abdominis activation — force
closure of SIJ; primary stabilisation exercise
Clamshell (band) 3 × 20 reps Gluteus medius — reduces SIJ shear via
posterior oblique sling system
Inner Range Quad (SLR in slight flexion) 3 × 15 reps VMO + hip stabiliser activation — reduce
pelvic instability
Side-lying Hip Abduction 3 × 20 reps Anterior oblique and posterior oblique sling
activation
Walking (short, even surfaces) 15 min × 2/day Controlled loading — avoid prolonged
single-leg stance phases
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Pain £3/10 with daily activities
• SIJ provocation tests reducing in severity
• Able to stand single-leg 10 sec without SIJ pain
Phase 2 — Sling System Strengthening (Week 4–12)
n GOALS
• Anterior and posterior oblique sling strength
• Lumbopelvic stability restoration
• Normalise gait and functional movement patterns
• Return to full daily activities
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid heavy asymmetric loading until sling strength
restored
• No single-leg heavy loading until Phase 3
• Monitor SIJ belt weaning — reduce gradually as
strength improves
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 16
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Dead Bug (SIJ focus — posterior pelvic tilt
maintained)
3 × 10 reps TA co-contraction with limb movement —
force closure maintained throughout
Bridge + Hip Abduction (clamshell at top) 3 × 15 reps Posterior oblique sling — glute max +
contralateral latissimus dorsi
Side Plank (progression to abduction at top) 3 × 30 sec each Anterior oblique sling — obliques +
contralateral hip adductors
Hip Thrust (heavy) 3 × 15 reps Glute max loading — key posterior sling
strengthening exercise
Pallof Press 3 × 12 reps each Rotational SIJ stability under controlled load
Step-up (lateral and forward) 3 × 15 reps each Single-leg loading progression — monitor
SIJ pain response
Walking (progressive) 30 min daily Gait normalisation — minimise pelvic drop
(Trendelenburg)
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Single-leg stance ³30 sec without SIJ pain
• Trendelenburg sign negative
• Full return to daily activities
Phase 3 — Return to Full Activity (Week 12–20)
n GOALS
• Return to sport, manual work, running
• Full pelvis and lumbopelvic load tolerance
• SIJ belt independence
n PRECAUTIONS
• SIJ injection if inadequate response at 3 months
• Radiofrequency denervation or prolotherapy for
chronic cases
• Maintain sling program long-term — SIJ dysfunction
recurs without exercise
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Single-leg Romanian Deadlift 4 × 10 reps Posterior sling under unilateral load —
master form before heavy loading
Lateral Lunge 4 × 12 reps each Frontal plane hip loading — SIJ
compression in adduction
Running Progression Stage program Begin when single-leg hop pain-free —
straight then curves
Sport-Specific Training Progressive sessions Gradual return — continue SIJ belt for first 4
weeks of sport
Maintenance Sling Program 3 × per week Lifelong exercise habit — SIJ relies on
muscle not ligament for stability
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• SIJ belt-free for all activities
• Running 20 min pain-free
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 17
• Full sport/work demands tolerated
REFERENCES & EVIDENCE BASE
• Vleeming A et al. (2012). European guidelines for the diagnosis and treatment of pelvic girdle pain. Eur Spine J.
• Laslett M. (2008). Evidence-based diagnosis and treatment of the painful sacroiliac joint. J Man Manip Ther.
• Simopoulos TT et al. (2012). A systematic evaluation of prevalence and diagnostic accuracy of sacroiliac joint interventions.
Pain Physician.
• Prather H. (2003). Sacroiliac joint pain: practical management. Clin J Sport Med.

64.
Piriformis Syndrome / Sciatic Nerve Compression
Phase 1 — Acute Symptom Management (Week 0–3)
n GOALS
• Reduce posterior buttock pain and sciatic referral
• Offload sciatic nerve from piriformis compression
• Identify and correct contributing factors (prolonged
sitting, hip IR weakness)
• Distinguish from lumbar disc radiculopathy (no spinal
flexion deficit in piriformis)
n PRECAUTIONS
• Rule out lumbar disc herniation (MRI if uncertain)
• Avoid prolonged sitting on hard surfaces —
compresses piriformis on sciatic nerve
• No aggressive piriformis stretching initially if acutely
inflamed
• No direct pressure massage on piriformis in acute
phase
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Piriformis Stretch (figure-4 / supine) 3 × 45 sec each Pull knee toward opposite shoulder —
gentle sustained stretch; most important
exercise
Hip Internal Rotation AROM (seated) 3 × 20 reps Restore hip IR — often limited; reduces
piriformis tension
Sciatic Neural Mobilisation (slider) 3 × 10 reps each Ankle DF + hip flexion — gentle nerve
gliding; avoid provocative tension
Prone Hip Extension (isometric) 3 × 15 reps, 5 sec hold Glute max activation — reduce piriformis
overload
Cryotherapy / Heat 15 min × 3–4/day Heat reduces piriformis spasm; ice reduces
nerve irritation — alternate based on
symptoms
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Buttock pain £3/10
• Leg symptoms (sciatic referral) reducing
• Able to sit 30 min without significant pain increase
Phase 2 — Strengthening & Neural Mobilisation (Week 3–10)
n GOALS
• Restore hip ER and abductor strength balance
• Resolve sciatic nerve irritability
• Normalise gait — reduce excessive femoral IR
loading
• Return to full daily activities
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid prolonged sitting without movement breaks
• Do not aggressively stretch if neural symptoms
worsen
• Progress hip loading based on sciatic symptom
response
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 19
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Clamshell (band) 3 × 20 reps Gluteus medius — reduce compensatory
piriformis activation as hip ER
Hip Abduction — standing (band) 3 × 20 reps Functional glute med loading — reduce
Trendelenburg-related piriformis stress
Hip Thrust 3 × 15 reps Glute max — reduce piriformis
overactivation as hip extensor substitute
Figure-4 Stretch Progression 3 × 60 sec each Deeper piriformis loading as acute
symptoms resolve — seated then standing
Sciatic Nerve Tensioner (progressive) 3 × 10 reps Seated slump ® standing SLR — increase
neural mobility
Single-leg Balance 3 × 45 sec each Hip stabiliser endurance — reduce dynamic
piriformis overuse
Walking (progressive) 30 min daily Gait correction — maintain hip ER and
abductor control throughout gait cycle
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Sciatic referral fully resolved
• Piriformis FAIR test negative or minimally positive
• Hip ER strength ³80% symmetry
Phase 3 — Return to Activity (Week 10–20)
n GOALS
• Full return to sport and occupational activities
• Hip strength symmetry ³90%
• No recurrence with prolonged sitting or running
n PRECAUTIONS
• Piriformis injection (corticosteroid or botulinum toxin)
if conservative management fails at 3 months
• Maintain ergonomic seated posture long-term
• Runners: gait retraining to reduce hip IR during
stance phase
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Single-leg Squat (hip ER control) 4 × 12 reps Maintain neutral hip alignment — prevent
excessive IR loading on piriformis
Running Gait Retraining Per physio assessment Increase cadence 5–10%, reduce hip
adduction — reduce piriformis compression
Lateral Band Walks 3 × 20 steps each Hip ER and abductor endurance —
piriformis unloading exercise
Sport-Specific Training Progressive sessions Return to full demands — include hip
strengthening as warm-up
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Running 30 min pain-free
• No sciatic referral with any activity
• Full sport participation

Cervical Facet Joint Pain
Phase 1 — Pain Control & Mobility (Week 0–3)
n GOALS
• Reduce cervical facet joint pain (typically unilateral
neck/suboccipital, worse with extension and ipsilateral
rotation)
• Restore segmental cervical mobility
• Correct forward head and rounded shoulder posture
• Patient education — highly responsive to
conservative management
n PRECAUTIONS
• Rule out upper cervical instability (C1–C2 — Down
syndrome, RA, trauma) before mobilisation
• Avoid end-range ipsilateral rotation and extension in
acute phase
• No high-velocity manipulation if acute nerve root
involvement
• Screen for vertebral artery insufficiency before
cervical manipulation
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Cervical Retraction (chin tucks) 3 × 15 reps, 5 sec hold Reduce cervical lordosis — offloads
posterior facet joints; primary postural
correction
Cervical AROM (rotation, lateral flexion) 3 × 15 reps each Pain-guided — move into comfortable
range; restore full ROM progressively
Scapular Retraction + Depression 3 × 20 reps, 5 sec hold Reduce upper trapezius dominance and
cervical facet loading
Heat + Soft Tissue Therapy 10–15 min pre-exercise Reduce periarticular muscle spasm —
improve mobility of treatment session
Pillow Adjustment Education Immediate Correct sleeping position — avoid
excessive cervical extension or lateral
flexion overnight
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Pain £3/10 with daily activities
• Cervical rotation ³60° bilaterally
• Morning stiffness under 20 min

n GOALS

n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid heavy overhead pressing with active cervical

Whiplash Associated Disorder (WAD Grade II)
Phase 1 — Acute Management (Week 0–3)
n GOALS
• Reduce acute cervical pain and muscle guarding
• Reassurance and positive prognosis messaging
• Maintain mobility — avoid collar immobilisation
• Screen for yellow flags (catastrophising,
fear-avoidance)
n PRECAUTIONS
• WAD Grade III (neurological deficit) or IV (fracture)
require imaging and specialist referral
• Avoid hard cervical collar — worsens outcomes; soft
collar max 72h only
• Screen for upper cervical instability post-MVA
• Early psychological support for distress significantly
improves prognosis
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Cervical AROM — all planes (immediate) 3 × 10 reps each direction Early movement is the single most
important intervention — prevents chronic
WAD
Cervical Retraction (chin tucks) 3 × 15 reps, 5 sec hold Restore natural cervical curve — reduce
posterior facet and ligament tension
Shoulder Shrugs + Rolls 3 × 15 reps Reduce upper trapezius spasm —
normalise shoulder girdle mechanics
Heat Application 15 min × 3–4/day Reduce paraspinal muscle spasm —
improve mobility
Graded Activity + Return to Normal Activities Daily Stay active at work and home — prolonged
rest increases chronicity risk
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Pain £4/10 with daily activities
• Cervical ROM recovering — especially rotation
• Yellow flags screened and addressed
Phase 2 — Active Rehabilitation (Week 3–10)
n GOALS
• Restore full cervical ROM
• Deep cervical flexor strength
• Reduce pain sensitisation and fear-avoidance
• Return to full work and driving
n PRECAUTIONS
• WAD has high risk of chronicity — address
psychosocial factors early
• Avoid passive treatments only — active participation
is essential
• Opioid medications contraindicated for chronic WAD
— increase sensitisation

Osteoporotic Vertebral Fracture (Conservative)
Phase 1 — Protection & Pain Management (Week 0–6)
n GOALS
• Fracture healing via protected activity — avoid
further compression
• Acute pain control
• Prevent deconditioning and bed-rest complications
• Medical management: calcium, vitamin D,
bisphosphonate review
n PRECAUTIONS
• AVOID all spinal flexion exercises — dramatically
increases vertebral fracture risk
• No lumbar rotation under load
• Thoracic brace (TLSO) may be prescribed — wear
as directed
• Monitor neurological status — cord compression is
rare but urgent if present
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Breathing Exercises (diaphragmatic) 5 min × 4/day Maintain chest expansion — prevents
pneumonia with limited mobility
Ankle Pumps + Lower Limb AROM 10 reps every hour DVT prevention — critical in acute fracture
with reduced mobility
Isometric Gluteal Sets 3 × 20 reps Maintain hip extensor activation without
spinal loading
Hip Extension in Standing (gentle) 3 × 15 reps Posterior chain activation — neutral spine
maintained; no forward lean
Gentle Walking (with brace if prescribed) 10 min × 2–3/day Weight bearing — essential for bone
healing; prevents osteoporosis progression
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Pain £4/10 with walking
• Fracture clinically stable at 6-week review
• Neurological status intact
Phase 2 — Extension Strengthening (Week 6–16)
n GOALS
• Spinal extensor and paraspinal strength (protects
vertebral body in extension)
• Restore functional mobility — avoid flexion-loaded
movements
• Reduce kyphosis progression
• Fall prevention — critical in osteoporotic population
n PRECAUTIONS
• NO spinal flexion exercises for 12 months
post-fracture minimum
• No abdominal crunches, sit-ups, or any trunk flexion
under load — ever
• Avoid carrying weight > 5 kg initially
• Fall prevention paramount — hip protectors, home
hazard assessment

n GOALS
• Maximise bone density via targeted loading
• Reduce fracture recurrence risk
• Full functional independence
• Long-term fall prevention program
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid ALL spinal flexion exercises lifelong
• Annual DEXA scan monitoring
• Review medications with GP — bisphosphonates,
hormone therapy, calcium/Vit D
• Second fracture risk very high — bone protection is
lifelong priority

Progressive Resistance Training (extension

weight-bearing
Multifactorial Fall Prevention Weekly program Balance, vision check, medication review,
home hazards — reduces fracture risk by
30%

Scheuermann's Kyphosis (Conservative)
Phase 1 — Postural Awareness & Flexibility (Week 0–6)
n GOALS
• Increase thoracic mobility and reduce fixed kyphosis
progression
• Strengthen thoracic extensors and scapular
retractors
• Patient education — bracing most effective during
growth spurt (Risser grade 3 or less)
• Reduce pain (typically interscapular or
thoracolumbar junction)
n PRECAUTIONS
• Milwaukee or Boston-Thoracic brace if curve >45°
and skeletal immaturity — orthopaedic management
• Avoid heavy spinal flexion loading (rowing, deadlift
with rounding)
• No intensive spinal extension in severe rigid
kyphosis — may cause pain at thoracolumbar junction
• Surgery considered for curves >70–75° with
neurological deficit or refractory pain
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Thoracic Extension (foam roller / towel roll) 3 × 10 reps, 5 sec hold Extend over foam roller at apex of kyphosis
— key mobilisation exercise
Prone Back Extension (passive and active) 3 × 10 reps Gravity-assisted thoracic extension —
progress to active extension hold 5 sec
Pectoralis Major Stretch (doorway) 3 × 45 sec Anterior chest tightness perpetuates
kyphosis — critical to address
Chin Tuck + Cervical Retraction 3 × 15 reps Correct compensatory forward head posture
— improve sagittal alignment
Standing Postural Awareness Hourly cue Wall stand: head, shoulders, hips, heels
touching — proprioceptive correction
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Thoracic extension mobility improving
• Postural awareness in standing and sitting
• Pain £2/10 with daily activities
Phase 2 — Targeted Strengthening (Week 6–20)
n GOALS
• Thoracic extensor and scapular retractor strength
• Core stability in extension-neutral spine
• Reduce kyphotic progression in skeletally immature
patients
• Return to full sport and recreational activity
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid exercises that increase thoracic flexion under
load (rowing with kyphosis, overhead carry)
• Bracing compliance critical in growing patients —
16–23h per day for best results
• Gymnastics, swimming backstroke, and extension
sports are beneficial

• Kyphosis stabilises at skeletal maturity — exercise

• Maintain upper back strengthening lifelong —

• Maintenance program independently performed

Thoracic Disc Herniation
Phase 1 — Acute Management (Week 0–4)
n GOALS
• Reduce thoracic pain and intercostal / girdle referral
• Identify directional preference (extension or flexion
relief)
• Neurological monitoring — thoracic disc can cause
myelopathy
• Patient education — most resolve with conservative
management
n PRECAUTIONS
• URGENT: Myelopathy signs (bilateral lower limb
weakness/spasticity, gait disturbance, bladder/bowel)
— emergency neurosurgical referral
• Thoracic disc herniation is rare (0.5% of disc
herniations) — rule out other causes
• Avoid thoracic manipulation if significant cord
compromise suspected
• MRI is investigation of choice — CT myelogram if
MRI contraindicated
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Thoracic AROM (rotation + extension in pain-free
range)
3 × 10 reps each Symptom-guided movement — identify
directional preference early
Prone Lying (passive extension) 3 × 5 min Extension bias if symptoms centralise —
McKenzie approach adapted for thoracic
spine
Neural Mobilisation (intercostal / thoracic) 3 × 10 reps Trunk side bend away from painful side —
gentle neural gliding
Thoracic Extension (foam roller — mid-thoracic) 3 × 10 reps Segmental mobilisation at disc level —
avoid if worsens symptoms
Walking (paced) 15–20 min × 2/day Controlled axial loading — better than rest;
monitor for myelopathic gait change
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Pain £4/10 with daily activities
• No progressive neurological symptoms
• Directional preference identified
Phase 2 — Stabilisation & Strengthening (Week 4–12)
n GOALS
• Thoracic extensor and core strength
• Full thoracic ROM pain-free
• Return to full daily activities and work
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid heavy axial loading (barbell squat, deadlift)
until 12 weeks
• No contact sport until neurological status confirmed
clear
• Monitor for symptom recurrence with loading

Post-Laminectomy Rehabilitation
Phase 1 — Immediate Post-operative (Week 0–4)
n GOALS
• Wound healing and pain management
• Begin controlled ambulation — same day or day 1
post-op
• DVT prevention
• Patient education: lifting restrictions, posture, activity
modification
n PRECAUTIONS
• No lifting >2 kg for first 4–6 weeks
• No sustained sitting >30 min initially
• No bending, twisting, or heavy exertion during
healing phase
• Monitor wound — infection, CSF leak, haematoma
are surgical emergencies
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Ankle Pumps (immediate post-op) 10 × every hour DVT prevention — begin in recovery room
Walking (hospital corridors — same day) 5–10 min × 3–4/day Early mobilisation is safe and improves
outcomes — avoid sitting to standing
repeatedly
Log Roll Technique (bed mobility) Every position change Protect operative site — engage core
before movement, no trunk twisting
Diaphragmatic Breathing 5 min × 4/day Prevent atelectasis — especially if epidural
or respiratory compromise
Quad Sets + Ankle Pumps 3 × 20 reps Maintain lower limb activation while mobility
limited
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
• Independent ambulation on flat with or without device
• Wound healed — sutures/staples removed at 10–14 days
• Pain £4/10 with walking
Phase 2 — Core Activation & Mobility (Week 4–10)
n GOALS
• Gradual lumbar core stabilisation
• Restore lumbar ROM — especially flexion
• Nerve root pain resolving
• Return to light work and ADLs
n PRECAUTIONS
• Avoid heavy lifting until 8–12 weeks post-op
• No return to manual labour until 12 weeks minimum
• Monitor for recurrent disc herniation — foot drop,
saddle anaesthesia are emergencies
• Scar tissue management at wound — soft tissue
mobilisation week 4–6

21. Hip Labral Tear (Conservative)
Phase 1 — Pain Reduction & Activity Modification (Week 0–6)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce intra-articular pain and inflammation
•
Identify and eliminate provocative loading positions (end-range flexion, IR)
•
Restore pain-free range of motion within safe arcs
•
Patient education — natural history, activity modification, postural correction
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid deep hip flexion beyond 90° (provocative for anterior labrum)
•
No pivoting or cutting — high torsional stress on labrum
•
Avoid hip external rotation combined with extension (prone lying)
•
Monitor for clicking, locking or giving way — may indicate displaced labral tear requiring surgical review
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Supine Hip AROM (pain-free arcs) 3 × 20 reps each direction Maintain synovial fluid circulation — avoid impingement zone (90° flexion + IR) Isometric Hip Abduction (side-lying) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec hold Activate gluteus medius without joint compression — sub-maximal effort Isometric Hip Extension (prone, neutral) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec hold Gluteus maximus activation — avoid extension past neutral Core Activation (TrA drawing-in) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec hold Restore deep stabiliser function — foundation for hip stability Stationary Bike (seat height high) 20–30 min Low hip flexion arc — cardiovascular maintenance without labral provocation Aquatic Walking / Pool Therapy 20 min Offloaded functional movement — excellent pain-free early option
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Pain ≤3/10 with daily activities and exercise
•
No clicking, locking, or giving way with walking
•
Hip AROM improving in pain-free range
Phase 2 — Stabilisation & Progressive Strengthening (Week 6–16)
■ GOALS
•
Restore hip abductor, extensor, and external rotator strength
•
Normalise lumbopelvic motor control and gait mechanics
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app
Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 3
•
Progressive closed-chain loading within safe range
•
Return to full ADLs and low-impact recreational activities
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid deep squat (>90° hip flexion) until pain-free at 90°
•
No high-impact loading or pivoting sports yet
•
Continue to monitor for labral symptoms with load progression
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Side-lying Hip Abduction (progressive) 3 × 20 reps Gluteus medius — key for frontal plane stability and reducing labral shear Clamshell (with resistance band) 3 × 20 reps External rotator + gluteus medius loading — progress band resistance weekly Bridge (isometric → dynamic) 3 × 15 reps Posterior chain activation — gluteus maximus + hamstrings in neutral pelvis Single-leg Bridge 3 × 12 reps each Unilateral glute max — reveals and corrects pelvic drop (Trendelenburg) Mini Squat / Wall Sit (0–60°) 3 × 15 reps / 3 × 30 sec Controlled closed-chain loading — avoid impingement zone Step-up (lateral) 3 × 15 reps each Functional gluteus medius — cue level pelvis throughout Hip Hinge (Romanian Deadlift, light) 3 × 12 reps Hip extensor loading pattern — critical for sport and daily function Single-leg Balance 3 × 45 sec each Proprioception restoration — progress to foam/perturbation
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Hip abductor strength ≥75% symmetry (dynamometry or manual testing)
•
Single-leg balance ≥45 sec without pelvic drop
•
Pain ≤2/10 with all phase 2 exercises
Phase 3 — Return to Sport & Function (Week 16–24+)
■ GOALS
•
Full return to sport, running, and recreational activities
•
Hip strength symmetry ≥90% (LSI)
•
Long-term self-management — recurrence prevention
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Surgical referral if symptoms persist beyond 3–4 months without improvement
•
Avoid return to pivoting/contact sport before strength and hop tests meet criteria
•
10% weekly mileage increase rule for running return

Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Full Squat (progressive depth) 4 × 10 reps Progress range as symptoms allow — full range when pain-free at 90° Lateral Band Walks 3 × 20 steps each Functional glute med loading — maintain hip neutral throughout Single-leg Romanian Deadlift 4 × 10 reps each Posterior chain unilateral strength — high specificity for sport mechanics Walk-Run Progression Stage program, 10% weekly Flat surface first — introduce change of direction at week 20 if pain-free Lateral Bounds (bilateral → unilateral) 3 × 10 reps Frontal plane loading — land softly with gluteus medius control Agility and Cutting Drills Progressive sessions Controlled direction change — monitor for labral provocation Sport-Specific Training Progressive sessions Graduated return with technique coaching

Running 30 min pain-free

22. Total Hip Arthroplasty (THA)
Phase 1 — Acute Post-operative (Week 0–6)
■ GOALS
•
Safe mobilisation with assistive device — achieve FWB as tolerated
•
Wound healing and oedema management
•
DVT prevention — early mobilisation and ankle pumps
•
Patient education — THA precautions (posterior or anterior approach-specific)
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
POSTERIOR APPROACH: No hip flexion >90°, no IR, no adduction past midline
•
ANTERIOR APPROACH: No hip extension past neutral, no ER in extended position
•
Anticoagulation per medical team — DVT risk high post-THA
•
Monitor wound for haematoma or infection
•
No pivoting on operative leg — rotate whole body when turning
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Ankle Pumps 10 reps every hour DVT prevention — begin immediately post-op; critical first 6 weeks Quad Sets (isometric) 3 × 20 reps Maintain quadriceps activation while NWB or PWB Heel Slides (supine) 3 × 20 reps Restore hip and knee flexion within precaution limits Hip Abduction (supine, within limits) 3 × 20 reps Gluteus medius activation — avoid adduction past midline Supported Standing + Weight Shift 3 × 5 min Early WB re-education with frame or crutches Gait Training (frame → crutches) Daily sessions Achieve safe FWB with correct gait pattern and precautions maintained Stair Training (one step at a time) Supervised daily Good leg up first, bad leg down first — patient must know this rule
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
FWB with single assistive device (one crutch / stick)
•
Wound healed — sutures/staples removed
•
Hip ROM improving within precaution limits
Phase 2 — Progressive Strengthening (Week 6–12)
■ GOALS
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app
Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 6
•
Restore hip abductor and extensor strength — eliminate Trendelenburg gait
•
Progress to unassisted ambulation on all surfaces
•
Normalise stair ascent and descent
•
Begin progressive closed-chain strengthening
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
THA precautions remain until surgeon formally clears (typically 6–12 weeks)
•
Avoid high-impact loading — no running yet
•
Monitor for hip pain, clicking, or instability with loading
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Side-lying Hip Abduction 3 × 20 reps Gluteus medius — primary Trendelenburg correction exercise Standing Hip Abduction (resistance band) 3 × 20 reps Functional closed-chain abductor loading Bridge (bilateral) 3 × 15 reps Gluteus maximus — progress to single-leg bridge when bilateral easy Sit-to-Stand (chair raised initially) 3 × 15 reps Functional quadriceps + glute max — progress chair height down over time Mini Squat / Step-up (week 8+) 3 × 15 reps Progressive closed-chain loading — maintain hip in neutral alignment Stationary Bike (seat high) 20 min Hip ROM and cardiovascular — begin week 6 when cleared; high seat limits flexion Hydrotherapy / Pool Walking 20 min Offloaded gait training — excellent bridge to unsupported land walking
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Walking independently without assistive device
•
No Trendelenburg sign at self-selected walking speed
•
Hip abductor strength ≥70% symmetry
Phase 3 — Return to Function (Week 12–26)
■ GOALS
•
Full lower limb strength symmetry ≥80%
•
Return to recreational activities: walking, golf, cycling, swimming
•
Long-term joint preservation — activity modification education
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
High-impact sport (running, racquet sports, football) generally discouraged long-term — discuss with surgeon
•
Low-impact activities: golf, swimming, cycling, doubles tennis — generally permitted at 6 months
•
Maintain healthy weight — each kg excess loads THA components significantly

■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Walking 30 min without pain or assistive device
•
Lower limb strength ≥80% symmetry

Surgeon clearance for recreational activity at 6-month review

25. Hamstring Strain (Grade II)
Phase 1 — Acute Protection & Early Loading (Week 0–2)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce pain and haematoma formation
•
Maintain tissue mobility — prevent excessive scar formation
•
Early pain-free active range of motion
•
Patient education — avoid passive stretching in reactive phase
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
DO NOT aggressively stretch hamstring in first 2 weeks — risk of re-tear and nerve involvement
•
Crutches if antalgic gait — normalise as soon as tolerated
•
Monitor for proximal hamstring avulsion (unable to flex knee against gravity)
•
Grade II involves partial tear — return to sport typically 4–8 weeks
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Prone Knee Flexion (AROM, pain-free) 3 × 20 reps Maintain hamstring muscle activation without tension on tear site Isometric Hamstring (submaximal) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec, 50–60% effort Early loading — pain ≤3/10; analgesic and anti-atrophy effect Hip Hinge (body weight only) 3 × 10 reps Gentle eccentric loading in safe range — avoid end-range knee extension Calf Raises + Ankle Pumps 3 × 20 reps Maintain distal muscle activity and circulation — DVT prevention if NWB Bike (low resistance) 15–20 min Cardiovascular maintenance — knee ROM maintained throughout pedal cycle RICE Protocol / Compression 48–72h Reduce haematoma — ice 15 min × 4/day in first 48h; compression bandage
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Walking pain-free without antalgic gait
•
Isometric strength 70% or greater of contralateral side
•
Pain ≤3/10 during all Phase 1 exercises
Phase 2 — Progressive Eccentric Strengthening (Week 2–6)
■ GOALS
•
Progressive hamstring loading using eccentric protocol
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app
Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 9
•
Restore full knee extension ROM within pain tolerance
•
Running preparation — straight-line jogging without pain
•
Re-establish neuromuscular control and inter-limb symmetry
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Apply 24h pain rule to guide load progression
•
Monitor for biceps femoris proximal pain — may indicate proximal hamstring tendinopathy complication
•
No high-speed sprinting or kicking until strength criteria met
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Nordic Hamstring Curl (modified → full) 3 × 6–8 reps, slow eccentric Gold standard eccentric load — proven recurrence reduction; begin partial ROM Prone Hamstring Curl (with resistance) 3 × 15 reps, slow eccentric Isolated eccentric hamstring loading — progress weight weekly Romanian Deadlift (bilateral) 3 × 12 reps Functional eccentric lengthening — posterior chain loading Single-leg Deadlift (bodyweight) 3 × 10 reps each Unilateral loading and balance — reveals inter-limb asymmetry Walk-Jog Progression Stage program Begin straight-line jogging week 3–4 when strength >70% symmetry Hip Flexor and Quadriceps Stretching 3 × 45 sec each Reduce anterior pelvic tilt — decrease hamstring overload pattern Single-leg Bridge 3 × 12 reps each Gluteus maximus loading — inter-muscular coordination restoration
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Hamstring strength ≥80% symmetry (dynamometry preferred)
•
Jogging 15 min continuously without pain or limp
•
Full knee extension ROM — no end-range tightness
Phase 3 — Return to Sport (Week 6–10)
■ GOALS
•
Full sprint speed and change-of-direction tolerance
•
Hamstring strength LSI ≥90%
•
Return to full training and competition
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Recurrence risk is highest at return to sport — do not rush criteria
•
Continue Nordic hamstring program 2× per week as permanent maintenance
•
10% weekly increase in sprint intensity and volume

26. Greater Trochanteric Pain Syndrome (GTPS)
Phase 1 — Load Reduction & Isometric Loading (Week 0–6)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce lateral hip pain and tenderness over greater trochanter
•
Identify and modify compressive load provocations (adduction, crossing legs, hip drop)
•
Isometric loading for analgesia
•
Patient education — tendon continuum model; avoid stretching into hip adduction
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
AVOID hip adduction past neutral — sitting cross-legged, lying on side with top leg drooping
•
AVOID standing hip stretch (pulling foot to opposite shoulder) — compresses gluteal tendons
•
AVOID running on cambered roads (downhill side leg in adduction)
•
Corticosteroid injection offers short-term relief but inferior long-term outcomes vs. loading exercise
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Isometric Hip Abduction (wall push) 5 × 45 sec, 70–80% effort Foot on wall pushing outward — immediate analgesic effect; key first-line treatment Isometric Hip Extension (prone) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec hold Gluteus maximus activation — maintain posterior chain without compression Supported Single-leg Stance (rail) 3 × 45 sec each Gluteus medius loading under controlled conditions — avoid Trendelenburg Stationary Bike (seat height correct) 20–30 min Cardiovascular maintenance — low gluteal tendon compressive load Sleeping Position Education Daily Side-lying: pillow between knees; avoid adducted position in bed
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Pain ≤3/10 during isometrics
•
Morning stiffness <20 min
•
Provocative positions identified and successfully modified
Phase 2 — Progressive Tendon Loading (Week 6–14)
■ GOALS
•
Tendon and gluteal muscle structural adaptation via heavy slow resistance
•
Hip abductor strength symmetry ≥80%
•
Normalise gait — eliminate hip drop (Trendelenburg) in walking
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app
Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 12
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
24h pain rule — soreness allowed during/after exercise, must resolve within 24h
•
Avoid excessive hip adduction loading (wide step-downs, steep side slopes)
•
No plyometric loading until end of phase 2
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Side-lying Hip Abduction (band/weight) 4 × 15 reps, slow tempo Heavy slow resistance — 3-sec concentric, 4-sec eccentric; key loading exercise Standing Hip Abduction (cable/band) 4 × 15 reps Functional closed-chain abductor loading — progress resistance weekly Single-leg Squat (0–60° range) 3 × 12 reps each Gluteus maximus + medius co-activation — cue lateral hip control; mirror feedback helpful Step-up (lateral, then forward) 3 × 15 reps each Functional hip abductor eccentric control — slow lowering phase Lateral Band Walk 3 × 20 steps each Continuous gluteus medius loading in functional plane Romanian Deadlift (bilateral → single-leg) 3 × 12 reps Posterior chain loading — functional hip hinge pattern for sport/work Gait Retraining Walking sessions Cue lateral trunk lean away from stance leg — reduce adduction angle and tendon compression
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Hip abductor strength ≥80% symmetry
•
Single-leg squat with minimal pelvic drop
•
Walking 30 min pain-free
Phase 3 — Return to Running & Sport (Week 14–24)
■ GOALS
•
Full running and sport tolerance without lateral hip pain
•
Hip abductor LSI ≥90%
•
Long-term recurrence prevention — lifelong load management program
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
10% weekly mileage increase rule
•
Avoid cambered road running — flat surfaces preferred
•
Continue compression avoidance strategies permanently (sitting posture, sleep position)

42. Femoroacetabular Impingement (FAI) — Conservative
Phase 1 — Pain Reduction & Movement Optimisation (Week 0–8)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce intra-articular pain and inflammation
•
Identify and modify impingement-provoking positions (hip flexion + IR + adduction — FADIR)
•
Restore pain-free ROM within impingement-free arc
•
Patient education — Cam vs. Pincer morphology, activity modification strategies
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid FADIR position: hip flexion >90° + internal rotation + adduction (classic impingement provocateur)
•
Avoid deep squatting and prolonged sitting with hip flexed beyond 90°
•
No high-impact sport until pain controlled and movement quality restored
•
Surgical referral (hip arthroscopy) if conservative treatment fails at 3–4 months — discuss early
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Hip AROM (impingement-free arcs) 3 × 20 reps each direction Maintain ROM in pain-free range — avoid end-range hip flexion with IR Posterior Capsule Stretching (if tight) 3 × 45 sec each Tight posterior capsule → anterior femoral head migration → impingement Isometric Hip External Rotation 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec hold Maintain external rotator strength — prevent IR-dominant movement pattern Core Stability (dead bug, modified) 3 × 10 reps each Improve lumbopelvic control — reduce anterior pelvic tilt that increases impingement Stationary Bike (seat high) 20–30 min Maintain cardiovascular fitness — high seat limits provocative hip flexion arc Aquatic Exercise / Pool Walking 20 min Offloaded mobility and fitness maintenance — excellent early option
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Pain ≤3/10 with daily activities
•
Impingement-provoking activities identified and modified
•
Hip ROM improving in pain-free range
Phase 2 — Strengthening & Movement Retraining (Week 8–16)
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app
Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 15
■ GOALS
•
Restore hip abductor, extensor, and ER strength in impingement-free range
•
Retrain movement patterns to reduce FADIR provocation
•
Return to low-impact sport and recreational activities
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Squat depth: limit to pain-free range — typically 60–80° hip flexion
•
Cue external rotation bias during all closed-chain exercises
•
Monitor for groin pain with loading — may indicate labral tear component
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Side-lying Hip Abduction 3 × 20 reps Gluteus medius — critical for frontal plane hip control Clamshell with resistance 3 × 20 reps External rotator loading — reduces IR-dominant impingement pattern Bridge + Single-leg Bridge 3 × 15 reps Gluteus maximus — posterior chain activation in neutral pelvis Step-up (controlled depth) 3 × 15 reps each Functional closed-chain loading within impingement-free range Hip Hinge / RDL (bilateral) 3 × 12 reps Posterior chain loading — hip-dominant pattern preferred over knee-dominant squat Lateral Band Walk 3 × 20 steps each Continuous glute med loading — maintain ER throughout Gait Retraining (step width, trunk lean) Walking sessions Widen step width and lean trunk toward stance leg — reduce FAI provocation during walking
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Hip abductor strength ≥80% symmetry
•
Pain ≤2/10 during all phase 2 exercises
•
Movement quality: no excessive IR or anterior pelvic tilt in squatting and stair tasks
Phase 3 — Return to Sport (Week 16–24+)
■ GOALS
•
Full return to sport including cutting and pivoting
•
Hip strength LSI ≥90%
•
Long-term activity modification and strength maintenance
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
If symptoms persist beyond 4 months of conservative treatment — surgical referral warranted
•
Deep squatting sports (powerlifting, wrestling) may require permanent technique modification
•
Maintain external rotation bias and core stability as permanent strategy

43. Hip Flexor Strain (Iliopsoas)
Phase 1 — Acute Management (Week 0–2)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce pain and muscle guarding in anterior hip / groin
•
Maintain hip ROM without aggravating torn fibres
•
Early pain-free muscle activation to reduce atrophy
•
Patient education — mechanism, typical 2–6 week recovery for Grade I–II
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid passive hip extension stretching in acute phase — risks extending tear
•
Avoid resisted hip flexion above 90° (provocative position for iliopsoas)
•
Crutches if antalgic gait — normalise as soon as pain permits
•
Rule out iliopsoas avulsion (ASIS or lesser trochanter fracture) in adolescents and powerful athletes
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Isometric Hip Flexion (submaximal) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec, 50% effort Maintain muscle activation without excessive tension on injured tissue Supine Hip AROM (flexion, pain-free range) 3 × 20 reps Prevent muscle shortening and maintain joint mobility Core Activation (TrA drawing-in, supine) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec hold Stabilise lumbar spine — reduce iliopsoas compensatory overload Stationary Bike (low resistance, low seat) 15–20 min Maintain cardiovascular fitness — low-load hip flexion arc Cryotherapy + Compression 15 min × 3–4/day Reduce pain and swelling in acute phase
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Walking pain-free without antalgic gait
•
Pain ≤3/10 during isometric hip flexion
•
No pain at rest
Phase 2 — Progressive Strengthening (Week 2–6)
■ GOALS
•
Progressive iliopsoas and hip flexor strengthening
•
Restore full hip ROM including extension
•
Return to jogging and linear running

44. Adductor / Groin Strain
Phase 1 — Acute Phase & Isometric Loading (Week 0–2)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce medial groin pain and muscle guarding
•
Early pain-free isometric loading — prevent atrophy
•
Identify and modify provocative activities
•
Patient education — groin strain vs. sports hernia vs. osteitis pubis — differentiation
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid aggressive passive stretching into hip abduction in reactive phase
•
Crutches if antalgic gait
•
Rule out pubic stress fracture, avulsion fracture (ASIS/pubic ramus) in young athletes
•
Chronic groin pain (>3 months) may require imaging to exclude sports hernia or hip joint pathology
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Isometric Adduction (Copenhagen position, submaximal) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec, 50% effort Pain-free adductor activation — analgesic and anti-atrophy; key early exercise Supine Hip Adduction (squeeze pillow) 3 × 20 reps, 5 sec hold Isolated adductor loading without weight-bearing stress Calf Raises + Ankle Pumps 3 × 20 reps Circulatory maintenance — circulation in acute phase Stationary Bike (low resistance) 15–20 min Cardiovascular maintenance — low adductor load Cryotherapy + Compression 15 min × 3–4/day Acute inflammation management — first 48–72h

Walking pain-free without antalgic gait
•

45. Piriformis Syndrome
Phase 1 — Pain Reduction & Neural Desensitisation (Week 0–4)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce deep gluteal pain and sciatic nerve irritation
•
Identify and modify provocative activities (sitting, running on cambered surfaces, hip IR)
•
Piriformis and deep external rotator mobility
•
Patient education — distinguish from true lumbar radiculopathy; often missed diagnosis
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Exclude lumbar disc herniation and true sciatica — piriformis syndrome is a diagnosis of exclusion
•
Aggressive piriformis stretching may irritate sciatic nerve in acute phase — gentle only
•
Prolonged sitting is primary provocation — modify chair height, add cushion, take movement breaks
•
Assess for leg length discrepancy, excessive foot pronation — contributing factors
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Piriformis Stretch (figure-4 supine) 3 × 30 sec each, gentle Hip ER + flexion — gentle sustained stretch; avoid sharp nerve pain during hold Sciatic Nerve Slider (supine) 3 × 10 reps each Ankle DF/PF with extended knee — gentle neural mobilisation; NOT if acute radicular pain Hip AROM (pain-free range) 3 × 20 reps each direction Maintain hip mobility — avoid provocative IR in acute phase Cryotherapy to Gluteal Region 15 min × 3/day Reduce local inflammation — piriformis deep to gluteus maximus Sitting Modification + Movement Breaks Every 20–30 min Avoid sustained compression of piriformis — key early intervention
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Deep gluteal pain ≤3/10 with sitting 20+ min
•
No acute sciatic radiation during daily activities
•
Piriformis stretch tolerated without nerve pain
Phase 2 — Strengthening & Biomechanical Correction (Week 4–10)
■ GOALS
•
Restore deep external rotator and gluteal strength
•
Correct contributing biomechanical factors (excessive foot pronation, weak hip abductors)
•
Return to walking, low-impact sport, and recreational activities
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app

physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 24
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid aggressive neural stretching — piriformis syndrome often involves neural sensitisation
•
Running on camber continues to be avoided until strength criteria met
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Clamshell (progressive resistance) 3 × 20 reps each Hip ER + abduction — targets piriformis and deep rotators Side-lying Hip Abduction 3 × 20 reps Gluteus medius — corrects hip adduction loading pattern Single-leg Bridge 3 × 12 reps each Gluteus maximus + posterior chain — reduce piriformis compensatory overload Deep External Rotator Stretching (progress) 3 × 45 sec Progress from figure-4 to pigeon pose as neural symptoms resolve Sciatic Nerve Mobilisation (progressive) 3 × 10 reps each Progress from slider to tensioner — seated slump → standing when tolerated Single-leg Squat (controlled) 3 × 12 reps each Unilateral lower limb loading — reveals and corrects dynamic valgus and IR Foot Orthosis / Pronation Control Daily footwear Reduce excessive IR transmitted up kinetic chain to piriformis

Sitting tolerance ≥45 min without pain

•
Full return to running and sport
•
Long-term sitting tolerance without recurrence
•
Self-management program established
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Return to road running: avoid cambered surfaces long-term
•
Sitting modification strategies maintained permanently at work/desk

46. Hip Osteoarthritis (Conservative)
Phase 1 — Pain Management & Education (Weeks 0–8)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce pain and improve quality of life
•
Comprehensive patient education — OA is manageable, not 'bone on bone inevitable surgery'
•
Identify and modify pain-provoking activities while maintaining movement
•
Establish walking and gentle exercise as primary long-term intervention
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid high-impact loading (running, jumping) during acute pain flares
•
Manage patient expectations — OA cannot be 'cured' conservatively but can be well-controlled
•
Orthopaedic referral if severe pain, radiographic Kellgren-Lawrence Grade III–IV, or loss of function refractory to conservative treatment
•
NSAIDs, topical diclofenac — appropriate adjunct; not a substitute for exercise
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Hydrotherapy / Aquatic Exercise 30 min, 3× per week Offloaded exercise — ideal for high-pain-severity OA; superior for early phase Stationary Bike 20–30 min Low-impact cardiovascular — hip ROM maintenance with minimal joint loading Seated Hip AROM 3 × 20 reps each direction Maintain synovial fluid circulation and prevent joint stiffening Gentle Walking (paced) 20–30 min daily Best evidence for hip OA — graded activity is the primary intervention Education: Weight Management Ongoing 5–10% weight reduction significantly reduces hip joint load and pain Heat Therapy 20 min before exercise Increase soft tissue extensibility — reduce pre-exercise stiffness
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Pain controlled to ≤4/10 during exercise
•
Walking 20 min without significant pain increase
•
Patient engaged with self-management education
Phase 2 — Progressive Strengthening (Weeks 8–24)
■ GOALS
•
Hip and quadriceps strength improvement — key for pain reduction and function in OA
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app
Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 27
•
Improve walking speed, stair function, and community mobility
•
Reduce pain and improve WOMAC and HOOS scores
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Pain should not exceed 5/10 during exercise (tolerable exertion level)
•
Progress load gradually — OA cartilage has limited tolerance for rapid load increases
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Sit-to-Stand (chair strengthening) 3 × 15 reps, slow lowering Quadriceps + glute max functional loading — progress by lowering chair height Hip Abduction (standing, band) 3 × 20 reps each Gluteus medius — reduce Trendelenburg and lateral hip stress Bridge + Single-leg Bridge 3 × 15 reps Posterior chain — progression as tolerated Mini Squat (pain-free range) 3 × 15 reps Closed-chain loading — maintain pain ≤4/10 throughout Step-up (progressive height) 3 × 15 reps each Functional lower limb loading — mirrors daily life demands Walking Program (progressive) 30–45 min daily Grade up weekly — best long-term OA intervention Swimming or Aquatic Aerobics 30 min, 2–3× per week Maintains cardiovascular fitness with minimal joint loading
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO MAINTENANCE
•
Walking 45 min without limiting pain
•
Stair use with reciprocal pattern and controlled descent
•
WOMAC pain sub-score improved ≥30%
Phase 3 — Long-term Maintenance (Month 6+)
■ GOALS
•
Lifelong self-management — exercise as medicine
•
Sustain strength and function gains achieved in Phase 2
•
Delay progression to THA through active lifestyle
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Exercise is safe and beneficial for hip OA — do not stop exercising during mild flares, modify intensity instead
•
Annual review — monitor radiographic and functional progression
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Progressive Resistance Training 2–3 × per week lifelong Hip abductors, extensors, quadriceps — the definitive OA intervention

47. Snapping Hip Syndrome (Coxa Saltans)
Phase 1 — Pain Reduction & Load Modification (Week 0–4)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce pain associated with snapping (internal: iliopsoas; external: IT band; intra-articular: labral tear)
•
Identify type of snapping — internal (groin snap) vs. external (lateral hip snap) vs. intra-articular (locking/giving way)
•
Modify provocative activities
•
Patient education — snapping alone without pain is benign; pain with snapping requires treatment
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Intra-articular snapping (with locking or giving way) — urgent orthopaedic review for labral tear
•
Avoid provocative hip movements (external: hip flexion-IR arc; internal: leg swing in walking)
•
Reduce repetitive hip flexion activities (hill running, stairs, dance) in acute painful phase
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Hip AROM (pain-free arcs, slow) 3 × 20 reps each Controlled movement through range — identify snap arc and avoid it initially Iliopsoas Isometric (for internal type) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec hold Maintain muscle activation without provocative snap movement IT Band / Hip Abductor Stretching (external type) 3 × 45 sec each Reduce IT band tension over greater trochanter — lateral tightness contributes Stationary Bike (low resistance) 20 min Maintain fitness — controlled hip flexion arc without provocative snap Cryotherapy (if painful snap) 15 min × 3/day Reduce local bursal inflammation if present (trochanteric bursitis component)
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Painful snapping episodes reduced in frequency
•
Pain ≤3/10 during daily activities
Phase 2 — Targeted Strengthening (Week 4–12)
■ GOALS
•
INTERNAL TYPE: Iliopsoas progressive loading to reduce tendon subluxation
•
EXTERNAL TYPE: IT band tissue loading + gluteus medius strengthening to reduce lateral snap
•
Restore full hip ROM through snap arc without pain

■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Snap may persist during rehab — target is pain-free snap, then eventual reduction in snap frequency
•
External type: avoid direct IT band compression (foam rolling directly on ITB is debated — prefer hip abductor strengthening)
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Resisted Hip Flexion (band, progressive) 3 × 15 reps (internal type) Progressive iliopsoas loading — reduces tendon subluxation with strength adaptation Side-lying Hip Abduction (band) 3 × 20 reps (external type) Gluteus medius — key for IT band tension reduction over greater trochanter Standing Hip Flexion (cable/band) 3 × 15 reps each Functional iliopsoas loading — begin at 50% effort, progress weekly Single-leg Squat 3 × 12 reps each Gluteus maximus + medius — frontal plane control reduces external snap Step-up (forward and lateral) 3 × 15 reps each Functional closed-chain loading — hip and knee alignment focus Lateral Band Walk 3 × 20 steps each IT band and gluteus medius — reduces lateral tension causing external snap Core Stability (dead bug, side plank) 3 × 10–45 reps/sec Lumbopelvic control — reduces compensatory hip flexor overload
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Pain-free with provocation of snap (snap may still occur)

Hip flexor or abductor strength ≥80% symmetry
•
Walking and stairs pain-free

■ GOALS
•
Return to dance, running, sport with snap frequency reduced
•
Full functional strength symmetry ≥90%
•

•
Surgical referral (iliopsoas lengthening or IT band release) only if symptoms persist >6 months of conservative treatment
•
Intra-articular causes — arthroscopic debridement or labral repair indicated if conservative management fails

23. Lateral Epicondylalgia (Tennis Elbow)
Phase 1 — Isometric Loading & Load Management (Week 0–4)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce lateral elbow pain and tenderness over lateral epicondyle
•
Isometric wrist extension for immediate analgesia
•
Identify and modify provocative activities (gripping, lifting, mousing)
•
Forearm ergonomic assessment — workplace, racquet sports
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid stretching wrist flexors in reactive phase — increases tensile load on ECRB
•
Reduce grip-intensive activity by 50%
•
Lateral epicondylitis brace (counterforce) may provide symptomatic relief — evidence is modest
•
Corticosteroid injection provides short-term relief but inferior long-term outcomes vs. exercise
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Isometric Wrist Extension (table edge) 5 × 45 sec, 70–80% effort Immediate analgesic effect — elbow at 90°, forearm pronated, wrist extended against table Isometric Grip (dynamometer or ball) 5 × 45 sec Maintain hand and forearm activation without provocative movement Wrist AROM (pain-free) 3 × 20 reps each direction Maintain joint mobility — avoid resisted extension in reactive phase Nerve Mobilisation (radial nerve slider) 3 × 10 reps each Elbow extension + wrist flex + shoulder ER — radial nerve often sensitised in LE Bike / Swimming 20–30 min Cardiovascular maintenance — minimal forearm loading
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Pain ≤3/10 during isometrics
•
Morning stiffness <15 min
•
Provocative activities identified and modified

■ GOALS
•
Tendon and ECRB structural adaptation via progressive loading

Wrist extensor strength symmetry ≥80%

24. Distal Radius Fracture (Post-immobilisation)
Phase 1 — Post-immobilisation Mobilisation (Week 0–4 post-cast removal)
■ GOALS
•
Restore wrist ROM — flexion, extension, pronation, supination
•
Reduce post-immobilisation oedema and stiffness
•
Restore grip strength (typically reduced 30–50% post-immobilisation)
•
Patient education — bone healing complete at 6–8 weeks; soft tissue recovery takes 3–6 months
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid aggressive passive wrist stretching for first 4 weeks post-cast removal
•
Complex Regional Pain Syndrome (CRPS) — monitor for disproportionate pain, allodynia, vasomotor changes
•
Scaphoid injury or TFCC tear — review imaging; separate protocols may apply
•
No resistive loading until wrist AROM is 50%+ of contralateral side
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Wrist AROM — all planes 3 × 20 reps each direction Flexion, extension, radial and ulnar deviation — pain-guided, gentle Forearm Rotation (pronation/supination) 3 × 20 reps each Often most restricted post-cast — critical for hand function Tendon Gliding Exercises (hand) 3 × 10 reps each position Hook fist, full fist, tabletop, straight — maintain finger tendon mobility in cast Grip Exercises (soft ball/putty, light) 3 × 20 reps Gradually restore grip function — putty provides graded resistance Oedema Management (elevation + retrograde massage) Several times daily Reduce distal oedema — maintain above heart level when not exercising Contrast Bathing (warm/cold) 2 × daily Reduce stiffness and pain — alternate warm and cool water immersion
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Wrist AROM >50% of contralateral side
•
Oedema significantly reduced
•
Grip strength beginning to improve — progressing each session
Phase 2 — Strengthening & Functional Restoration (Week 4–12)
■ GOALS
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app
Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Page 36
•
Restore wrist and forearm strength to functional levels
•
Return to two-handed functional tasks (cooking, writing, driving)
•
Grip strength ≥70% of contralateral side
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Avoid heavy loading (>2 kg) until 8 weeks post-fracture minimum
•
Monitor for CRPS — any worsening pain, colour changes, or temperature asymmetry requires medical review

36. Medial Epicondylalgia (Golfer's Elbow)

•

37. UCL Sprain — Elbow (Ulnar Collateral Ligament)
Phase 1 — Acute Protection & Early Mobilisation (Week 0–4)
■ GOALS
•
Protect medial elbow from valgus stress — ligament healing
•
Reduce pain and inflammation
•
Maintain elbow ROM within pain-free range
•
Patient education — Grade I–II: conservative success rate 75–80%; Grade III: Tommy John surgery consideration
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
ABSOLUTE: No throwing or valgus loading until Phase 3 clearance
•
Hinged elbow brace may be used in Grade II tears (30–90° limit) for 4–6 weeks
•
Monitor for ulnar nerve symptoms (medial elbow tingling/ring-little finger weakness) — cubital tunnel complication
•
Surgical referral: Grade III tears in overhead athletes — Tommy John (UCL reconstruction) highly successful
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Elbow AROM (flexion/extension, neutral forearm) 3 × 20 reps Restore elbow ROM — avoid valgus stress; no carrying weight Forearm Pronation/Supination 3 × 20 reps each Maintain forearm rotation — important for function Wrist and Hand AROM 3 × 20 reps each Prevent distal stiffness during elbow protection phase Isometric Elbow Flexion/Extension (submaximal) 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec hold Maintain muscle activation without valgus stress Cryotherapy + Compression 15 min × 3–4/day Acute inflammation management — especially first 72h Shoulder and Scapular Maintenance 3 × 15 reps Prevent proximal deconditioning — shoulder ER, scapular retraction
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Pain ≤3/10 at rest and with AROM
•
Full elbow AROM within brace limits (if worn)
•
No ulnar nerve symptoms
Phase 2 — Strengthening (Week 4–12)

38. Carpal Tunnel Syndrome (Conservative)
Phase 1 — Neural Desensitisation & Symptom Control (Week 0–6)
■ GOALS
•
Reduce median nerve compression symptoms — numbness, tingling, night pain
•
Wrist splinting in neutral — primary conservative intervention
•
Identify and modify provocative postures (wrist flexion/extension extremes, sustained gripping)
•
Patient education — CTS pathophysiology, activity modification, sleep positioning
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Urgent surgical referral: thenar muscle atrophy, constant numbness with motor weakness, failed 3–6 months conservative
•
Splint must hold wrist in neutral (0°) — NOT in extension (common error)
•
Avoid prolonged wrist flexion (cycling, sleeping curled position, driving)
•
Diabetes, hypothyroidism, pregnancy — treat underlying condition alongside physiotherapy
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Neutral Wrist Splinting Nightly + provocative activities Keeps carpal tunnel volume maximal — neutral wrist = lowest carpal canal pressure Median Nerve Slider 3 × 10 reps each Wrist neutral, extend fingers, oppose thumb, then extend wrist — gentle neural gliding Tendon Gliding Exercises 3 × 10 reps each position Hook fist, full fist, tabletop, straight — reduces adhesions around flexor tendons in canal Workplace Ergonomic Assessment Once (key intervention) Keyboard height, mouse position, wrist angle — major modifiable risk factor Median Nerve Tensioner (cautious) 3 × 10 reps (mild-moderate cases) Progress from slider when acute symptoms reduce — elbow extension + wrist/finger extension
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS
•
Night symptoms improving — better sleep quality
•
Daytime numbness/tingling reducing with splint and modification
•
No progression of thenar weakness
Phase 2 — Strengthening & Function Restoration (Week 6–16)
■ GOALS
•
Restore grip and pinch strength

•
Return to full occupational and recreational hand use
•

■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Continue night splinting until symptom-free — typically 3–6 months
•
If symptoms worsen with strengthening — return to Phase 1 and reassess for surgical candidacy
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Grip Strengthening (putty/dynamometer) 3 × 20 reps Gradually restore grip — monitor for symptom provocation Pinch Strengthening (opposition exercises) 3 × 20 reps Thenar muscle function — critical if weakness identified Wrist Flexor/Extensor Strengthening 3 × 15 reps each Forearm muscle balance — reduce compensatory neural tension Dexterity Training (pegboard, fine motor) Daily practice Restore fine motor function — important for work return Functional Task Progression Daily Typing, gripping, writing with wrist neutral — ergonomic technique Median Nerve Mobilisation (maintenance) Daily Continue neural mobilisation — maintain nerve tissue health

•

•

40. Scaphoid Fracture (Post-immobilisation)
Phase 1 — Post-cast Mobilisation (Week 0–4 post-cast removal)
■ GOALS
•
Restore wrist ROM — flexion, extension, radial/ulnar deviation, forearm rotation
•
Reduce post-immobilisation stiffness and oedema
•
Begin grip and pinch strengthening
•
Patient education — scaphoid healing: waist fracture takes 10–12 weeks+; proximal pole may require 6 months; AVN risk
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Confirm radiographic (CT preferred) union before progressing — scaphoid non-union is serious (AVN risk)
•
Proximal pole fractures: higher non-union risk, longer immobilisation — up to 16 weeks in cast
•
NEVER progress to resistive loading without confirmed healing on imaging
•
Surgical fixation (screw) allows earlier rehab — follow post-op protocol timeline

41. TFCC Injury — Triangular Fibrocartilage Complex (Conservative)

■ GOALS
•
Protect TFCC from provocative loading (rotation under load, ulnar deviation under load)
•

•

•
Patient education — TFCC classification: peripheral tears (better healing capacity) vs. central tears (avascular)
■ PRECAUTIONS
•
Ulnar-sided wrist brace (long or short opponens) — worn during all activities for 6 weeks
•
Avoid forearm rotation under load (carrying, twisting) — primary TFCC loading mechanism
•
MRI or wrist arthroscopy may be needed to classify tear — important for surgical planning if conservative fails
•
Peripheral TFCC tears (vascular zone) have high conservative healing success (~80%); central tears may require debridement

71. Cubital Tunnel Syndrome
Phase 1 — Conservative Management & Nerve Protection (Week 0–6)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Reduce ulnar nerve irritation at the cubital tunnel • Avoid sustained elbow flexion — limits nerve excursion
• Avoid provocative postures (sustained elbow flexion >90°) • No resting elbow on hard surfaces
• Patient education on nerve compression mechanisms • Nighttime elbow extension splint (0–30° flexion) — key
intervention
• Reduce intrinsic hand muscle weakness and sensory
symptoms
• Refer if progressive motor weakness, intrinsic wasting, or
EMG severity warrants surgical review
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Elbow Extension Splinting Nightly — 0–30° flexion Reduces cubital tunnel pressure — most effective
conservative intervention
Nerve Gliding — Ulnar Nerve Slider 3 × 10 reps each arm Shoulder abduction + elbow extension with wrist flexion
— gentle excursion, NOT if acute
Cervical ROM & Neural Tension Check 3 × 15 reps each direction Rule out double crush — cervical C8/T1 contribution to
ulnar symptoms
Intrinsic Hand Strengthening (putty) 3 × 20 reps, light resistance Maintain hand strength — finger abduction, adduction,
pinch
Posture Education & Ergonomic
Modification
Ongoing Avoid elbow flexion >90° at desk/phone — foam padding
over medial elbow
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Tingling symptoms reducing in ring and small fingers
3 Elbow flexion provocation test negative or improving
3 Nighttime splinting tolerated
3 Ready for progressive neural loading
Phase 2 — Neural Mobilisation & Strengthening (Week 6–12)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Progressive ulnar nerve mobilisation • Do not perform neural tensioners if acute paresthesia
worsens
• Restore full elbow ROM without symptom provocation • Avoid heavy carrying with elbow flexed
• Forearm and intrinsic hand muscle strength • Monitor for grip weakness progression
• Return to full daily activities
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Ulnar Nerve Slider (progression) 3 × 15 reps each Wrist extension + elbow extension + shoulder abduction
— progressive excursion
PhysioNutritionMSK Rehab Protocols physionutrition.vercel.app
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
Elbow Flexion/Extension AROM 3 × 20 reps full range Restore full elbow ROM — symptom-guided pace
Wrist Flexor/Extensor Strengthening 3 × 15 reps, light resistance Restore forearm muscle balance — reduce nerve
compression secondary to tightness
Grip Strengthening (dynamometer/putty) 3 × 20 reps Restore grip strength — monitor for fatigue asymmetry
Finger Abduction Resistance (paper
between fingers)
3 × 20 reps First dorsal interosseous — ulnar innervated key muscle
Scapular Stabilisation 3 × 15 reps Reduce thoracic outlet contribution — lower trapezius
and serratus anterior
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Paresthesia £ mild with full daily activity
3 Elbow flexion test negative at 60 seconds
3 Grip strength >75% of uninvolved side
3 Ready for return to full upper limb loading
Phase 3 — Return to Full Activity (Week 12–20)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Full grip and pinch strength symmetry • Surgical referral (anterior transposition) if symptoms
persist >6 months with conservative treatment
• Return to sport and manual work demands • Avoid repetitive elbow flexion-extension occupational
tasks without rest breaks
• Long-term nerve health self-management • Continue ergonomic modifications lifelong
• Prevent recurrence
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Progressive Resistance Training (upper
limb)
4 × 10 reps, progressive
load
Full upper limb strengthening — monitor for ulnar
symptom recurrence
Grip and Pinch Dynamometer Training 4 × 20 reps Symmetry target >90% — document progress with
objective measures
Sport-Specific Upper Limb Loading Progressive sessions Throwing, racquet sport, manual work — graduated
return
Ergonomic Reassessment Ongoing Workstation height, phone use, elbow padding —
permanent modification
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Grip strength ³90% of uninvolved side
3 No provoked paresthesia with full upper limb loading
3 Return to sport and work without restriction
REFERENCES & EVIDENCE BASE
• Bartels RH et al. (2009). Surgical treatment of ulnar nerve compression at the elbow. Cochrane Database Syst Rev.
