72. Radial Tunnel Syndrome
Phase 1 — Load Reduction & Pain Management (Week 0–4)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Reduce radial nerve irritation in the radial tunnel • Avoid resistive supination and radial deviation in acute
phase
• Differentiate from lateral epicondylalgia (tenderness 4–5
cm distal to lateral epicondyle)
• No forceful gripping with wrist in flexion and forearm in
pronation
• Identify and modify provocative activities (forearm
pronation, wrist flexion)
• Monitor for PIN palsy (posterior interosseous nerve) —
finger drop is surgical emergency
• Patient education on nerve vs tendon pathology • NSAIDs may assist in acute nerve irritation phase
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Rest from Provocative Activities Relative rest 2–4 weeks Identify top 3 provocative tasks — modify or eliminate
temporarily
Radial Nerve Slider 3 × 10 reps Elbow extension + wrist flexion + ulnar deviation —
gentle nerve mobilisation
Wrist Extensor Stretching (gentle) 3 × 30 sec each Reduce muscle belly compression on PIN —
symptom-guided
Cervical Screen (C5–C6 contribution) Clinical assessment Rule out cervical radiculopathy contributing to lateral arm
symptoms
Cryotherapy 15 min × 3–4/day Reduce local nerve irritation — lateral proximal forearm
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Pain £3/10 with resisted supination
3 Provocative activities modified
3 No signs of PIN palsy
3 Ready for progressive loading
Phase 2 — Progressive Strengthening (Week 4–10)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Restore forearm supinator and wrist extensor strength • Avoid heavy resistive supination until pain <3/10
• Progressive radial nerve mobilisation • Monitor for symptom radiation into posterior forearm with
loading
• Normalise upper limb biomechanics • Progress gradually — 24h pain rule applies
• Return to full daily activities
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Supination with Resistance (hammer
exercise)
3 × 15 reps, 3-sec eccentric Eccentric supinator loading — begin light, progress to
hammer/dumbbell
PhysioNutritionMSK Rehab Protocols physionutrition.vercel.app
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
Wrist Extension (eccentric focus) 3 × 15 reps, 4-sec eccentric ECRB/ECRL loading — progressive resistance band
Radial Nerve Slider (progression) 3 × 15 reps Add shoulder depression and contralateral cervical
lateral flexion
Forearm Pronation/Supination (dumbbell) 3 × 20 reps, slow tempo Full ROM forearm rotation — restore normal mechanics
Grip Strengthening 3 × 20 reps Restore grip symmetry — functional radial tunnel loading
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Resisted supination pain-free or £2/10
3 Wrist extension strength >75% symmetry
3 No forearm radiation with loading
3 Ready for sport/work-specific loading
Phase 3 — Return to Full Activity (Week 10–20)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Full forearm strength and endurance symmetry • Surgical decompression if no response at 6 months
• Return to sport, manual work, and repetitive upper limb
tasks
• Avoid sustained forearm pronation with wrist flexion in
occupational settings
• Long-term recurrence prevention • Gradual return to repetitive upper limb sports (tennis, golf)
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Progressive Forearm Strengthening 4 × 12 reps, progressive
load
Supination, pronation, wrist extension — symmetry target
³90%
Sport-Specific Loading (racquet, tools) Progressive sessions Graduated return — technique modification to reduce
radial tunnel stress
Ergonomic and Technique Coaching Ongoing Keyboard, power tools, sport technique — prevent
recurrence
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Strength symmetry ³90% supination and wrist extension
3 Return to full sport and work without lateral forearm pain
3 No radial nerve provocation with functional tasks
REFERENCES & EVIDENCE BASE
• Lister GD et al. (1979). The radial tunnel syndrome. J Hand Surg Am.
• Naam NH & Nemani S. (2012). Radial tunnel syndrome. Orthop Clin North Am.
• Huisstede BM et al. (2008). Incidence and prevalence of upper extremity musculoskeletal disorders. BMC Musculoskelet Disord.
• Rinker B. (2004). The posterior interosseous nerve compression test. J Hand Surg Am.

73. Trigger Finger (Post-injection / Post-op)
Phase 1 — Oedema Control & Early Mobilisation (Week 0–3)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Reduce post-procedural oedema and pain • Post-injection: avoid heavy gripping for 2–3 weeks
• Restore initial tendon glide through A1 pulley • Post-op: wound care and suture protection — no
submersion until healed
• Prevent adhesion formation • Avoid resisted finger flexion against resistance in first 2
weeks
• Patient education on tendon care post-procedure • Buddy taping not required unless adjacent digit instability
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Active Finger Flexion/Extension (tendon
gliding)
5 × 10 reps, hourly Hook fist ® straight fist ® full fist ® tabletop position —
4-position glide protocol
Oedema Management — Elevation +
Retrograde Massage
Continuous + 5 min × 3/day Elevate hand above heart — retrograde massage from
fingertip to palm
Intrinsic Stretching (MCP flexion, IP
extension)
3 × 20 reps Prevent intrinsic tightness — maintain full IP extension
Cryotherapy (palm, not wound) 10 min × 4/day Reduce post-procedural swelling — avoid direct ice over
incision
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Triggering absent or significantly reduced
3 Active finger flexion: full composite fist forming
3 Wound healed (post-op)
3 Ready for progressive loading
Phase 2 — Progressive Strengthening (Week 3–8)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Restore full finger flexion and extension ROM • Avoid heavy pinch and power grip until week 6
• Restore grip strength and dexterity • Monitor for triggering recurrence — report to surgeon if
returns
• Resume light daily activities • Scar management post-op from week 3 once healed
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Tendon Gliding (progressive resistance) 4 × 15 reps Add light resistance putty to glide sequence — build
flexor tendon load capacity
Putty Squeeze (cylindrical grip) 3 × 20 reps, light then
medium
Restore flexor digitorum superficialis and profundus
strength
PhysioNutritionMSK Rehab Protocols physionutrition.vercel.app
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
Pinch Strengthening (lateral + pulp) 3 × 20 reps Restore functional pinch for daily tasks — use pinch
dynamometer to measure
Scar Massage (post-op) 5 min × 3/day Silicone gel or massage — begin when wound fully
healed — prevent scar tethering
Dexterity Tasks (pegs, coins, writing) 10 min × 2/day Functional restoration — simulates daily task demands
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Full composite fist — fingertip to distal palmar crease
3 Grip strength >75% of uninvolved hand
3 No triggering with full flexion
3 Ready for return to full activity
Phase 3 — Return to Full Function (Week 8–16)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Full grip and pinch strength symmetry • Avoid sustained forceful gripping without rest breaks
• Return to sport, manual work, and fine motor tasks • Vibrating tool use — may provoke recurrence in stenosing
tenosynovitis
• Long-term recurrence prevention • Surgical referral if triggering recurs despite injection × 2
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Progressive Resistance (putty, spring
grippers)
4 × 20 reps Advance to firm putty and commercial grip trainers —
symmetry goal
Sport/Work-Specific Grip Tasks Progressive sessions Racquet sport, climbing, keyboard — graduated
exposure
Strengthening Maintenance Program 2–3 × per week Lifelong — prevent tendon sheath thickening recurrence
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Grip strength ³90% of uninvolved hand
3 Full active ROM — no triggering
3 Return to full occupational and recreational function
REFERENCES & EVIDENCE BASE
• Nimigan AS et al. (2006). Steroid injections in the management of trigger fingers. Am J Phys Med Rehabil.
• Sato ES et al. (2012). Treatment of trigger finger: randomised clinical trial comparing corticosteroid injection and NSAID. J Hand Surg Eur.
• Dierks R et al. (2012). Percutaneous trigger finger release. J Hand Surg Eur.

74. Mallet Finger
Phase 1 — Immobilisation (Week 0–8)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Continuous DIP joint extension splinting for tendon or
bone healing
• DIP joint must NOT be allowed to flex — even
momentarily — during splint changes
• Prevent extensor lag development • Stack splint or thermoplastic splint in 0° or slight
hyperextension
• Educate patient on strict splint compliance • If bony mallet >33% articular surface or volar subluxation
— surgical referral
• Monitor for skin complications under splint • Check skin integrity under splint at every session —
pressure sores risk
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
DIP Extension Splinting (continuous) 24 hours/day × 8 weeks Zero flexion tolerance — patient must maintain extension
with one hand while changing splint
PIP Joint AROM (uninvolved joints) 5 × 10 reps, hourly Maintain PIP and MCP mobility while DIP immobilised
Oedema Management (elevation,
retrograde massage)
Continuous + 5 min × 4/day Reduce swelling — facilitates splint compliance and
comfort
Skin & Splint Check Every 2–3 days Check for maceration, pressure necrosis — adjust splint
as needed
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 8 weeks continuous immobilisation completed
3 No extensor lag at DIP joint
3 Skin integrity maintained
3 Ready for graduated flexion introduction
Phase 2 — Graduated Mobilisation (Week 8–12)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Restore DIP flexion without lag recurrence • If extensor lag returns — resume continuous splinting
immediately
• Maintain full DIP extension • Night splinting for further 6 weeks after day weaning
• Restore tendon glide and finger dexterity • No heavy gripping or resisted DIP flexion until week 12
• Bony mallet: await radiographic healing confirmation
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Active DIP Flexion (protected range) 5 × 10 reps, hourly Begin 25° flexion — increase 10°/week guided by lag
assessment
PhysioNutritionMSK Rehab Protocols physionutrition.vercel.app
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
Passive DIP Flexion (gentle) 3 × 20 reps Restore joint mobility — do NOT force beyond comfort
Active DIP Extension (tendon
re-education)
3 × 20 reps Terminal extensor re-activation — ensure lag absent
before each session
Putty Grip (light) 3 × 15 reps Begin light tendon loading when lag <10°
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Full active DIP extension — zero lag
3 DIP flexion ³60°
3 No tendon re-rupture signs
3 Ready for progressive strengthening
Phase 3 — Return to Full Function (Week 12–20)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Full DIP ROM — extension 0° and flexion ³80° • Sport return: buddy tape DIP for contact sport 6 months
• Restore grip and pinch strength symmetry • Residual lag <10° is functionally acceptable in non-manual
workers
• Return to sport and work • Monitor for swan-neck deformity — PIP hyperextension
compensating for DIP lag
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Composite Fist and Tendon Gliding 4 × 15 reps Full 4-position tendon glide — restore full FDP excursion
Grip and Pinch Strengthening 4 × 20 reps Progressive putty and spring grippers — symmetry target
³90%
Sport/Work-Specific Tasks Progressive sessions Ball catching, keyboard, tools — sport-specific DIP
loading
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 DIP extension 0° — no lag
3 DIP flexion ³80°
3 Grip strength ³90% of uninvolved hand
3 Return to full sport and work
REFERENCES & EVIDENCE BASE
• Smit JM et al. (2010). Mallet finger: conservative versus operative treatment. J Hand Surg Eur.
• Handoll HH & Vaghela MV. (2004). Interventions for treating mallet finger injuries. Cochrane Database Syst Rev.
• Stack HG. (1969). A modified splint for mallet finger. J Hand Surg.

75. Boutonniere Deformity
Phase 1 — PIP Extension Splinting (Week 0–6)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Restore central slip integrity via PIP joint extension • PIP joint must be held in full extension 0° — continuous
splinting
• Allow lateral bands to migrate dorsally • DIP joint must be left FREE and actively flexed — critical
to treatment
• Prevent fixed boutonniere deformity • Acute traumatic boutonniere: 6 weeks continuous PIP
splinting
• DIP flexion exercises to passively stretch retinacular
ligament
• Surgical referral if fixed deformity >30° or failed
conservative management
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
PIP Extension Splinting (continuous) 0° — 24 hours/day × 6
weeks
Cylinder or gutter splint — maintain central slip
approximation
Active DIP Flexion (splint on) 5 × 10 reps, hourly Passively glides lateral bands dorsally — essential
component, not optional
MCP and uninvolved finger AROM 5 × 10 reps, hourly Prevent proximal joint stiffness during immobilisation
Oedema control — elevation and
retrograde massage
Continuous + 5 min × 4/day Reduce PIP joint swelling — facilitates splint compliance
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 6 weeks PIP extension splinting completed
3 Passive PIP extension 0° achieved
3 No fixed flexion contracture development
3 Ready for graduated PIP mobilisation
Phase 2 — Graduated PIP Mobilisation (Week 6–10)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Restore PIP flexion without deformity recurrence • Night splint PIP in extension for 6 further weeks
• Maintain full PIP extension — no lag • If boutonniere recurs — resume full-time splinting
• Restore extensor mechanism balance • Do not rush PIP flexion — central slip failure risk
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Active PIP Flexion (graduated) 5 × 10 reps, hourly Week 6: 30° flexion; week 7: 45°; week 8: 60° — guided
by deformity absence
Active PIP Extension re-education 3 × 20 reps Central slip activation — ensure full active extension
before flexion session
DIP Active Flexion (continuation) 3 × 20 reps Continue lateral band mobilisation through phase 2

PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
Tendon Gliding — Modified Protocol 3 × 15 reps Adapted glide sequence maintaining PIP extension at
end of each rep
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 PIP extension 0° — no lag
3 PIP flexion ³90°
3 No boutonniere pattern during active motion
3 Ready for progressive strengthening
Phase 3 — Strengthening & Return to Function (Week 10–16)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Full PIP and DIP ROM • Buddy tape PIP for contact sport for 3–6 months
• Restore grip and pinch strength • Residual PIP flexion contracture <30° — acceptable if
functional
• Return to sport and work • Surgical referral for fixed deformity not responding to
splinting
• Prevent deformity recurrence
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Composite Fist Tendon Gliding 4 × 15 reps Full tendon glide protocol — restore FDS, FDP, and
extensor balance
Grip and Pinch Strengthening (putty) 4 × 20 reps Progressive resistance — symmetry target ³90%
Sport/Work-Specific Tasks Progressive sessions Sport return with buddy tape, keyboard, manual work
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 PIP extension 0°, flexion ³100°
3 Grip strength ³90% of uninvolved hand
3 No deformity recurrence with full loading

76. Flexor Tendon Repair (Zone II)
Phase 1 — Early Protected Mobilisation (Week 0–4)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Prevent adhesion formation in critical Zone II ('no man's
land')
• Dorsal blocking splint: wrist 20° flexion, MCP 60–70°
flexion, IPs extended
• Early tendon gliding within dorsal blocking splint • No active finger flexion beyond dorsal blocking splint
boundaries
• Wound healing and oedema control • No resisted gripping — repair rupture risk highest first 4
weeks
• Protect repair from gap formation or rupture • Immediate referral if tendon re-rupture suspected (sudden
extension regain with loss of flexion)
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Passive Finger Flexion / Active Extension
(Kleinert)
5 × 10 reps, every 2 hours Rubber band traction flexes finger — patient actively
extends within splint limits
Place-and-Hold (modified Duran) 5 × 10 reps, every 2 hours Passively position finger in flexion — patient holds
isometrically for 5 sec
Tendon Glide (passive — all joints) 5 × 10 reps, every 2 hours Passive flexion through full composite fist — FDS and
FDP differential glide
Oedema Management (elevation +
retrograde massage)
Continuous + 5 min × 4/day Proximal to distal — reduce oedema to facilitate tendon
excursion
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 No signs of tendon rupture (sudden pain or snap with exercise)
3 Wound healing progressing
3 Passive composite fist achieved
3 Ready for early active motion (surgeon clearance at 4 weeks)
Phase 2 — Early Active Motion (Week 4–8)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Active FDS and FDP tendon glide • Begin active flexion only with surgeon clearance at week 4
• Differential gliding exercises to reduce adhesion formation • Avoid heavy resisted gripping until week 8
• Restore functional grip and dexterity • Monitor for adhesion signs: reduced active vs passive
ROM difference
• Buddy tape if adjacent digit at risk
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Active Composite Fist 5 × 10 reps, hourly FDP and FDS active flexion — ensure full DIP and PIP
flexion
PhysioNutritionMSK Rehab Protocols physionutrition.vercel.app
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app Evidence-Based Rehab
Hook Fist (isolated FDP) 5 × 10 reps, hourly DIP flexion with PIP extended — maximise FDP
excursion
Straight Fist (isolated FDS) 5 × 10 reps, hourly PIP flexion, DIP straight — maximise FDS excursion, key
for Zone II
Differential Tendon Gliding 4 × 15 reps, 4 positions Tabletop ® hook ® straight ® full fist — 4-position
protocol
Grip Strengthening (light putty) 3 × 20 reps from week 6 Begin light resistance — prevent atrophy without repair
overload
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Active composite fist ³75% total active motion (TAM)
3 No extensor lag
3 Grip strength >50% of uninvolved hand
3 Ready for progressive resistance
Phase 3 — Progressive Strengthening & Return to Function (Week 8–16)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Full TAM — total active motion symmetry • Avoid full power grip before week 10–12
• Restore grip and pinch strength ³90% • Tenolysis may be required if significant adhesions limit
TAM at 3 months
• Return to sport, manual work, and high-demand tasks • Contact sport: buddy tape for 6 months post-repair
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Progressive Putty Gripping 4 × 20 reps, progressive
resistance
Light ® medium ® firm — symmetry target ³90%
Resisted Finger Curls (dumbbell) 4 × 12 reps Full ROM flexion against resistance — FDP and FDS
loading
Dexterity and Fine Motor Tasks 10 min × 2/day Pegs, coins, typing, tools — functional TAM restoration
Sport/Work-Specific Loading Progressive sessions Climbing, racquet sport, manual work — progressive grip
demands
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 TAM ³90% of uninvolved hand
3 Grip strength ³90% of uninvolved hand
3 Return to full occupational and sport demands

77. Extensor Tendon Repair
Phase 1 — Immobilisation / Early Mobilisation by Zone (Week 0–4)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Protect extensor tendon repair per zone-specific protocol • Zone I–II (mallet): DIP splint 0° extension × 8 weeks (see
Mallet Finger protocol)
• Prevent repair rupture and lag formation • Zone III–IV (boutonniere): PIP splint 0° × 6 weeks (see
Boutonniere protocol)
• Control oedema and wound healing • Zone V–VIII (dorsal hand/wrist): Dynamic extension splint
or static wrist 40–45° extension
• Educate patient on zone-specific precautions • No resisted finger/wrist flexion in first 4 weeks — repair
rupture risk
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Zone V–VIII: Wrist/Finger Extension
Splinting
Wrist 40–45° extension,
MCPs at 0°
Static splint days; dynamic outrigger allows passive
flexion/active extension
Passive MCP Flexion (within splint) 5 × 10 reps, every 2 hours Dynamic outrigger protocol — elastic traction returns
finger to extension
Active MCP Extension (gentle) 5 × 10 reps, every 2 hours Within splint boundary — prevents adhesion formation

78. Dupuytren's Contracture (Post-surgical)

n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Wound healing post-fasciectomy or needle aponeurotomy • Extension splinting at night — begin day 1 post-op

surgically

80. Proximal Humerus Fracture (Non-operative)
Phase 1 — Protection & Pendular Exercises (Week 0–3)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Fracture protection via sling immobilisation • Sling immobilisation × 2–4 weeks (fracture type
dependent — 2-part minimal displacement vs 3-part)
• Reduce pain and muscle spasm • No active shoulder abduction or external rotation in first 3
weeks
• Prevent shoulder stiffness with early pendular motion • Vascular injury: axillary artery — assess radial pulse and
forearm perfusion
• Elbow, wrist, and hand ROM maintenance • Nerve injury: axillary nerve (deltoid) — assess lateral
shoulder sensation
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Pendular (Codman) Exercises 3 × 5 min, 3–4×/day Gravity-assisted — lean forward, arm hangs, small
circles and pendular motion — passive glenohumeral
motion
Elbow Flexion/Extension AROM 3 × 20 reps Prevent elbow stiffness — remove sling, support arm
Wrist and Hand AROM + Grip 3 × 20 reps Prevent distal stiffness and oedema
Cervical ROM 3 × 15 reps each direction Reduce secondary cervical muscle guarding —
ipsilateral neck pain common
Ice and Pain Management 15 min × 4–6/day Reduce regional pain and muscle spasm — facilitates
pendular motion compliance
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Pain £4/10 at rest
3 Fracture site not tender to direct palpation (clinical healing signs)
3 Radiographic evidence of early callus or fracture stability at 4–6 week X-ray
3 Ready for active-assisted shoulder motion
Phase 2 — Active-Assisted & Active ROM (Week 3–8)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Restore glenohumeral and scapulothoracic ROM • No lifting >1 kg until radiographic union confirmed
(typically week 6–8)
• Begin rotator cuff and scapular stabiliser activation • Avoid aggressive passive stretching — non-union risk in
poor bone quality (elderly)
• Progress from gravity-eliminated to gravity-resisted motion • AROM to pain-free range only — not into impingement arc

n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Reduce intramuscular haematoma and inflammation • AVOID massage in first 48–72 hours — myositis

hours

PRICE Protocol Ice 15 min × 4–6/day,
compression, elevation

hours

84. Gluteal Muscle Strain
Phase 1 — Acute Management (Day 0–7)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Reduce pain and haematoma in gluteal muscle belly • Avulsion of ischial tuberosity (proximal hamstring / gluteal)
— radiograph required; surgical referral if displaced
• Identify specific muscle involved (maximus, medius,
minimus) — determines loading strategy
• Grade II+: avoid active hip extension and abduction
loading in first 5 days
• Protect tear from extension • Rule out piriformis involvement — sciatic nerve irritation
possible
• Maintain cardiovascular fitness • PRICE protocol × 48–72 hours
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
PRICE Protocol Ice 15 min × 4–6/day,
compression
Reduce haematoma — compression shorts for deep
gluteal haematoma containment

Isometric Hip Abduction (side-lying) 3 × 20 reps, sub-maximal Glute medius activation — pain-free only
Stationary Bike (seated, low resistance) 15–20 min from day 3–5 Cardiovascular maintenance — minimal gluteal eccentric
load
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Pain £3/10 with walking
3 Gluteal palpation tenderness reducing
3 Hip extension and abduction pain-free at sub-maximal
3 Ready for progressive strengthening

n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Progressive gluteal loading — closed chain and open

• Avoid deep hip flexion stretch if proximal hamstring/glute

• Restore hip extension and abduction strength • Monitor for piriformis syndrome — seated pain and sciatic

• Normalise gait mechanics • 24-hour pain rule — guide load progression
• Return to jogging

Glute Bridge (bilateral ® single-leg) 4 × 15 reps Glute max loading in extension — progress to single-leg

85. Tibial Stress Fracture (Return to Running)
Phase 1 — Rest & Load Reduction (Week 0–6)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Eliminate tibial pain with daily activities • High-risk sites (anterior tibial cortex, medial malleolus,
navicular) — NWB and orthopaedic referral
• Allow bone healing — remodelling requires 4–8 weeks
minimum
• Posteromedial tibia (common site) — activity modification,
not full NWB unless severe
• Identify and correct predisposing factors (training errors,
nutrition, biomechanics)
• Rule out relative energy deficiency in sport (RED-S) —
especially female runners
• Maintain cardiovascular fitness with low-impact
alternatives
• Bone stress index screening: DEXA scan, blood panel (Vit
D, ferritin, hormonal status)
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale
Deep Water Running 30–45 min daily Zero impact cardiovascular — maintains VO2max and
running mechanics
Stationary Bike (no impact) 30–40 min daily Low tibial load cardiovascular maintenance
Swimming 30–45 min daily Zero tibial load — cardiovascular and mental health
Upper Body Strength Training 3–4 × per week Maintain neuromuscular fitness — seated exercises
Hip Strengthening (non-impact) 3 × 15 reps Gluteal and hip abductor strengthening — reduce tibial
loading on return
n CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE
3 Pain-free with all daily weight-bearing activities
3 No tibial pain with hop test (when clinically ready)
3 MRI or CT healing evidence (if initial imaging taken)
3 Predisposing factors identified and addressed
Phase 2 — Progressive Walking & Walk-Run (Week 6–10)
n GOALS n PRECAUTIONS
• Gradual tibial bone loading — progressive mechanical
stimulus for remodelling
• Pain during walking = return to Phase 1
• Restore normal walking mechanics before introducing
running
• 10% weekly mileage rule — absolute minimum
progression rate
• Begin return to running program on appropriate surface • Avoid hard surfaces initially — track or treadmill preferred
• Biomechanical assessment and footwear review • Avoid back-to-back running days until 30 min continuous
running tolerated

LOWER LIMB
86. Medial Tibial Stress Syndrome (Shin Splints)
Phase 1 — Load Reduction & Bone Stress Protection (Week 0–4)
■ GOALS • Reduce medial tibial pain and periosteal inflammation • Identify and modify training load provocations (mileage, surface, footwear) • Maintain cardiovascular fitness through low-impact alternatives • Patient education on bone stress continuum — MTSS to stress fracture spectrum
■ PRECAUTIONS • Exclude tibial stress fracture: point tenderness over cortex + imaging if ≥3 weeks no improvement • Cease running immediately if 'pinpoint' bony tenderness or positive hop test • Reduce training load by 50–80% — no increase until pain-free with walking • Avoid hard surfaces and hill running in acute phase • Footwear assessment: motion-control shoe or orthotic if overpronated
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Pool Running / Aqua Jogging 30–45 min, 5×/week Maintain cardiovascular fitness — zero tibial impact load Stationary Bike (low resistance) 30 min, daily Non-weight-bearing cardiovascular maintenance Calf Raises (bilateral, pain-free) 3 × 20 reps Maintain soleus and gastrocnemius activation — tibial stress reduction Tibialis Posterior Strengthening (band) 3 × 20 reps Key muscle for tibial stress absorption — arch support function Hip Abductor & Glute Med Strengthening 3 × 15 reps Reduce tibial internal rotation and pronation — biomechanical MTSS driver Calf Stretching (standing, bent knee) 3 × 45 sec each Reduce soleus tension — decrease tibial torsion stress Walking (pain-free distance only) As tolerated Maintain bone loading stimulus at sub-threshold level
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Pain ≤2/10 with walking 30 min on flat surface • Bony point tenderness resolved or significantly reduced • Stress fracture excluded clinically or via imaging
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
• Ready for graduated return-to-run protocol
Phase 2 — Graduated Return to Running (Week 4–12)
■ GOALS • Progressive tibial bone loading via structured run-walk protocol • Full calf, tibialis posterior, and hip abductor strength symmetry • Address biomechanical risk factors: overpronation, hip weakness, cadence • Normalise running gait mechanics — cadence optimisation (180 steps/min target)
■ PRECAUTIONS • Apply 10% weekly mileage increase rule — no skipping stages • Stop any run session if pain exceeds 3/10 — rest 2 days before retrying • Monitor for recurrence of medial tibial symptoms with loading • Introduce one new variable at a time (surface, speed, or volume — not all together)
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Walk-Run Intervals (Week 4–6) 3 min walk / 1 min run × 8 Gradual tibial bone loading — begin on soft surface (grass/track) Run-Walk Intervals (Week 6–8) 5 min run / 2 min walk × 5 Progress tibial loading stimulus — pain-guided progression Continuous Easy Run (Week 8–12) 20 → 30 min, 10% weekly increase Transition to sustained running — flat surface, controlled pace Single-leg Calf Raises (eccentric focus) 4 × 15 reps, slow 4-sec lowering Heavy slow eccentric loading — tibial shock absorption capacity Tibialis Posterior Resistance (progressive) 4 × 12 reps, band or weight Posterior tibial tendon — primary dynamic arch stabiliser Hip Strengthening Circuit 3 × 15 reps each Glute max, glute med, hip external rotators — reduce tibial torsion Running Cadence Training 180 steps/min target Higher cadence — reduces tibial bone stress by 8–12% Foam Rolling (calf and tibialis anterior) 2 × 60 sec each Myofascial release — reduce tibial periosteal tension
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Running 20 min continuously pain-free • Single-leg calf raise ≥90% symmetry • No medial tibial pain during or 24h after running • Ready for unrestricted sport return

physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
Phase 3 — Return to Full Sport & Recurrence Prevention (Week 12–20)
■ GOALS • Full unrestricted running at sport-specific pace and volume • Speed work and interval training reintroduced • Long-term load management education for recurrence prevention • Self-monitoring skills for early symptom recognition
■ PRECAUTIONS • Continue 10% weekly mileage increase rule for full season • Avoid sudden surface changes (track to road, grass to concrete) • Maintain strength training 2–3× per week throughout season • Risk of recurrence is high (75%) — ongoing load monitoring essential
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Tempo Runs (progressive) 10 min → 20 min, 85% HRmax Sport-specific intensity — introduce after 30 min easy run pain-free Interval Training (progressive) 4 × 400m → 8 × 400m High-intensity tibial loading — begin week 14–16 if pain-free Plyometric Program (bilateral → unilateral) 3 × 10 reps Energy storage capacity — tibial stiffness adaptation Strength Maintenance Program 2–3 × per week Eccentric calf raises, tibialis posterior, hip strengthening — non-negotiable Orthotics / Footwear Review Ongoing Custom orthotic or motion-control shoe — modify pronation biomechanics Load Monitoring Diary Weekly Subjective pain score + training load tracking — athlete self-regulation
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Running 45 min at race pace without pain • Hop test LSI ≥90% • Full sport-specific demands met without tibial symptoms

LOWER LIMB
87. Stress Reaction (Femoral Neck)
Phase 1 — Protected Weight Bearing & Bone Protection (Week 0–8)
■ GOALS • Prevent progression to complete femoral neck stress fracture • Non-weight-bearing or protected weight bearing per orthopaedic guidance • Maintain cardiovascular fitness and proximal muscle strength • Identify and address RED-S (Relative Energy Deficiency in Sport) if present
■ PRECAUTIONS • URGENT: Femoral neck stress fracture on tension side (superior) = surgical emergency — urgent orthopaedic referral • NWB mandatory if complete fracture or tension-side involvement on MRI • No single-leg stance, impact, or twisting until cleared by orthopaedic surgeon • Screen for RED-S: menstrual irregularity, low bone density, disordered eating • Bone health optimisation: calcium 1000–1500 mg/day, vitamin D 1500–2000 IU/day • MRI is gold standard for diagnosis and staging — X-ray often negative
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Pool Walking / Aqua Running 30–45 min daily Zero-impact cardiovascular fitness — fully offloaded in deep water Stationary Bike (seated) 30 min daily Low femoral neck load — maintain aerobic fitness Upper Body Conditioning As tolerated Seated rowing, upper body weights — prevent deconditioning Ankle Pumps (DVT prevention) 10 × every hour if NWB DVT prevention during restricted mobility phase Hip Abductor Isometrics (side-lying) 3 × 20 reps, 5 sec hold Maintain glute med activation without femoral neck loading Quad Sets & SLR (supine) 3 × 20 reps Maintain quadriceps strength in protected phase Core Stabilisation (supine, no hip flexion load) 3 × 10 reps Dead bug modified — no provocative hip positions
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Orthopaedic clearance for progressive weight bearing (typically 6–8 weeks) • MRI evidence of healing / reduction in bone marrow oedema • Pain ≤2/10 with protected ambulation
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
• No groin pain with gentle hip ROM
Phase 2 — Progressive Weight Bearing & Strengthening (Week 8–16)
■ GOALS • FWB progression over 2–4 weeks — guided by pain and imaging • Hip abductor and external rotator strength restoration • Core and lumbopelvic stability • Normalise gait pattern — eliminate antalgic gait
■ PRECAUTIONS • Advance WB only with surgeon clearance — follow MRI-guided protocol • No running until FWB without pain for minimum 2 weeks • Avoid Trendelenburg gait — indicates premature loading • Monitor for recurrent groin or thigh pain with WB progression
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Pool Walking (progressive depth) 20 min → 40 min Graduated weight bearing — waist-deep then thigh-deep Land Walking (flat surface) 10 → 30 min, pain-guided Progress when FWB pain-free — flat surface only initially Hip Abductor Strengthening (band) 3 × 20 reps Glute med — primary dynamic stabiliser of femoral neck loading Hip External Rotator Strengthening 3 × 15 reps Clamshell, prone hip ER — reduce femoral neck shear forces Bilateral Squat (bodyweight → loaded) 3 × 15 reps Begin when FWB pain-free — progress load gradually Single-leg Stance (stable surface) 3 × 30 sec Begin when bilateral squat pain-free — monitor Trendelenburg Hip Hinge (Romanian Deadlift, light) 3 × 12 reps Posterior chain loading — teach hip-dominant movement pattern
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • FWB without pain or assistive device • Normal gait on flat and stairs without Trendelenburg • Hip abductor strength ≥80% symmetry • MRI confirms adequate healing for return to impact
Phase 3 — Return to Running & Sport (Week 16–26)
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
■ GOALS • Graduated return to running via walk-run protocol • Full hip strength symmetry ≥90% • Sport-specific loading tolerance • Long-term bone health and recurrence prevention program
■ PRECAUTIONS • Minimum 4–6 months from injury before full sport return • Orthopaedic clearance mandatory before any running • Continue RED-S management — sports dietitian referral if indicated • Annual bone density monitoring (DXA) in high-risk athletes • 10% weekly mileage increase rule — strict adherence
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Walk-Run Protocol (Week 16–18) 3 min walk / 1 min run × 8 Begin on flat, soft surface — monitor groin pain response Progressive Running (Week 18–22) 10 → 30 min continuous Pain-guided progression — never exceed 3/10 pain Single-leg Hip Strengthening (full program) 4 × 12 reps each Hip abductors, extensors, external rotators — bilateral symmetry target Plyometric Progression (bilateral → unilateral) 3 × 10 reps Begin bilateral hops — monitor femoral neck pain response Sport-Specific Drills Progressive sessions Cutting, change of direction — introduce after 25 min run pain-free Bone Loading Program (impact) 2–3 × per week Controlled impact loading — supports bone remodelling and adaptation

UPPER LIMB
88. Pectoralis Major Tear (Conservative)
Phase 1 — Protection & Pain Management (Week 0–6)
■ GOALS • Protect healing pectoralis major fibres (typically musculotendinous junction) • Reduce pain, swelling, and bruising in anterior chest and axilla • Sling immobilisation for Grade II tears — 2–4 weeks • Patient education on healing timeline and functional expectations
■ PRECAUTIONS • Grade III (complete) tears with significant functional demand: surgical repair recommended — refer surgeon • No shoulder horizontal adduction or internal rotation beyond neutral for 4–6 weeks • No pushing, pressing, or lifting activities in acute phase • Monitor for neurovascular complications — axillary nerve/vessel proximity • MRI required for accurate grading and surgical decision-making
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Sling Immobilisation 2–4 weeks (Grade II) Protect torn fibres — maintain elbow at 90°, arm across body Pendulum Exercises (gravity-assisted) 3 × 20 reps Maintain GH joint mobility — pain-free range only Elbow & Wrist AROM 3 × 20 reps each Prevent distal stiffness during immobilisation Scapular Retraction (gentle) 3 × 15 reps Maintain scapular control — avoid pec major activation Grip Strengthening (hand exercises) 3 × 20 reps Maintain forearm and hand function during arm rest Cryotherapy 15 min × 4–6/day Haematoma and oedema management — critical first 72 hours
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Pain ≤3/10 at rest • Bruising and swelling resolving • Shoulder PROM >120° flexion and 90° abduction • Surgeon / physiotherapist clearance for progressive loading
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
Phase 2 — Progressive Strengthening (Week 6–16)
■ GOALS • Restore pectoralis major strength through progressive loading • Re-establish neuromuscular control of shoulder girdle • Full shoulder ROM restoration • Return to daily activities and light sport participation
■ PRECAUTIONS • No horizontal bench pressing until 12 weeks minimum • Avoid combined shoulder abduction + external rotation (maximally loads repaired tissue) • Progress load based on pain response — 24h pain rule applies • Monitor for asymmetry or contour deformity with loading
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Shoulder AROM (all planes, progressive) 3 × 20 reps each Restore full flexion, abduction, ER/IR — pain-guided progression Resisted Shoulder IR (band) 3 × 20 reps, slow tempo Isolated pec major activation via internal rotation — pain-free Resisted Shoulder Horizontal Adduction (band, limited range) 3 × 15 reps, 3-sec con/4-sec ecc Controlled pec loading — avoid end-range stretch across body Push-up (wall → incline, progressive) 3 × 15 reps Graduated pec major loading — wall first, reduce angle progressively Cable Chest Fly (mid-range only) 3 × 12 reps Controlled load through pec — avoid full horizontal abduction stretch Serratus Anterior Strengthening 3 × 15 reps Scapular protraction control — prevents shoulder impingement Scapular Stabilisation Program 3 × 15 reps each Lower and middle trapezius — restore force couple balance
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Full shoulder ROM equal to contralateral side • Pec major strength >70% symmetry (Cybex or manual assessment) • Push-up × 20 pain-free on flat surface • Ready for sport-specific and plyometric loading
Phase 3 — Return to Full Sport (Week 16–24)
■ GOALS • Pectoralis major strength symmetry ≥90%
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
• Full bench press and pressing movements tolerated • Sport-specific upper limb loading capacity • Long-term strength maintenance to prevent re-tear
■ PRECAUTIONS • Graded return to bench press: dumbbells before barbell • Recurrence risk with conservative management in complete tears — monitor • Collision sport contact: minimum 20–24 weeks from injury • Ongoing strength maintenance 2–3× per week throughout season
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Dumbbell Bench Press (progressive load) 4 × 10 reps Begin with light dumbbells — progress weekly within pain tolerance Barbell Bench Press (Week 18–20) 4 × 8–10 reps Introduce barbell when DB press pain-free at moderate load Incline and Decline Press (progressive) 4 × 10 reps Multi-vector pec loading — replicate sport-specific demands Dumbbell Chest Fly (full ROM) 3 × 12 reps Eccentric lengthening phase — restore full ROM under load Plyometric Push-up Progressions 3 × 10 reps Power development — medicine ball, clap push-ups progressively Sport-Specific Upper Limb Drills Progressive sessions Throwing, pushing, tackling — controlled introduction of sport loads Strength Maintenance Program 2–3 × per week Maintain pec major hypertrophy and eccentric capacity — prevent recurrence
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Pec major strength ≥90% symmetry • Full bench press with sport-specific load pain-free • Sport-specific demands met without pain or weakness

UPPER LIMB
89. Biceps Rupture — Distal (Post-op)
Phase 1 — Protection & Wound Healing (Week 0–6)
■ GOALS • Protect surgical repair of distal biceps tendon to radial tuberosity • Prevent re-rupture during critical early healing phase • Wound healing and oedema management • Maintain shoulder and wrist range of motion
■ PRECAUTIONS • Elbow flexion splint at 90° for first 2–4 weeks — per surgeon protocol • STRICT: No active elbow extension or passive elbow extension beyond 90° for 6 weeks • No supination against resistance for 6 weeks minimum • No lifting, pushing, or pulling in early phase • Monitor for lateral antebrachial cutaneous nerve symptoms (common surgical complication)
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Shoulder AROM (pendulum, AROM) 3 × 20 reps Maintain GH mobility — no elbow loading required Wrist & Finger AROM 3 × 20 reps Prevent distal stiffness during elbow immobilisation Elbow PROM (flexion, within splint range) 3 × 15 reps, gentle Prevent adhesions — passive motion within surgeon-specified range Elbow AROM Flexion (gravity-eliminated) 3 × 10 reps, gentle Begin when cleared week 3–4 — forearm supported Grip Strengthening 3 × 20 reps Maintain hand strength — no forearm rotation stress Cryotherapy & Elevation 15 min × 4/day Post-operative oedema and pain management
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Wound healed, sutures removed • Elbow AROM: 30–130° (within surgeon guidelines) • Surgeon clearance for progressive loading at 6-week review • No neurovascular complications
Phase 2 — Progressive Loading (Week 6–16)
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
■ GOALS • Full elbow ROM restoration — flexion, extension, pronation, supination • Progressive biceps tendon loading — begin isometrics, progress to isotonics • Forearm and supinator strength restoration • Return to light daily activities and ADLs
■ PRECAUTIONS • Supination loading later than flexion — higher stress on distal attachment • No heavy lifting (>2 kg) until week 10 • Avoid resisted eccentric loading until week 12 • Monitor for heterotopic ossification (radio-ulnar synostosis — rare but significant complication)
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Elbow AROM — Full Range 3 × 20 reps each Flexion, extension, pronation, supination — restore full ROM progressively Isometric Elbow Flexion (week 6–8) 3 × 10 reps, 5 sec Begin tendon loading — submaximal effort initially Resisted Elbow Flexion (band, week 8+) 3 × 15 reps, slow Progressive biceps loading — light band, 3-sec concentric/4-sec eccentric Resisted Supination (band, week 10+) 3 × 15 reps, slow Supinator and biceps — progress after flexion strength established Pronation / Supination Dumbbell (week 10+) 3 × 15 reps Functional forearm rotation loading — seated, supported Wrist Curls and Reverse Curls 3 × 15 reps Forearm flexor/extensor balance — support elbow recovery Shoulder Strengthening (rotator cuff) 3 × 15 reps Restore full shoulder-elbow kinetic chain — proximal stability
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Elbow ROM: full flexion, extension within 10° of contralateral • Pronation/supination: full ROM without pain • Biceps strength >60% symmetry • No heterotopic ossification on imaging
Phase 3 — Return to Full Function (Week 16–24)
■ GOALS • Biceps and forearm strength symmetry ≥90% • Return to manual work, sport, and gym training • Full weightlifting and heavy lifting tolerance
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
• Long-term recurrence prevention through strength maintenance
■ PRECAUTIONS • No maximum-effort supination or lifting until 20–24 weeks • Return to heavy manual labour: typically 4–6 months • Contact sport: minimum 6 months from surgery • Monitor for re-rupture signs: pop, sudden weakness, ecchymosis
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Dumbbell Bicep Curls (progressive) 4 × 10 reps Increase load weekly — target symmetry in peak force output Barbell Curl (Week 18+) 4 × 8 reps Introduce barbell when DB tolerated at moderate load Chin-ups / Assisted Chin-ups 4 × 8–10 reps Full-load supinated grip compound exercise — high biceps demand Supination Resistance Training (progressive) 4 × 12 reps Key functional test — supination strength for throwing and twisting tasks Eccentric Bicep Curls (21s protocol) 3 × 7 + 7 + 7 Full eccentric loading — prevent re-rupture via tendon adaptation Functional Upper Body Program 3 × per week Rowing, pulling, pressing — integrated upper limb loading Sport-Specific Return Program Progressive sessions Throwing, racquet sports, contact — graded exposure to demand
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Biceps strength LSI ≥90% (dynamometer) • Supination strength ≥90% symmetry • Heavy lifting and sport-specific demands pain-free

UPPER LIMB
90. Triceps Tendinopathy
Phase 1 — Isometric Loading & Load Management (Week 0–4)
■ GOALS • Reduce posterior elbow pain at olecranon insertion • Isometric triceps loading for immediate analgesia • Identify and modify provocative activities (throwing, pressing, dips) • Patient education on tendon continuum model
■ PRECAUTIONS • Avoid stretching triceps in reactive/pain phase — worsens irritation • Reduce pressing and overhead activities by 50–80% • Exclude posterior elbow pathology: olecranon bursitis, osteochondral lesion, valgus extension overload • Monitor for concomitant UCL stress in throwers — associated pathology
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Isometric Tricep Extension (elbow 60–70°) 5 × 45 sec, 70–80% effort Mid-range isometric — immediate analgesic effect; avoid full extension Isometric Tricep Push (wall, elbow 90°) 5 × 45 sec Submaximal isometric — analgesic stimulus for cortical pain inhibition Bike / Swimming (low arm load) 20–30 min Cardiovascular maintenance — minimal triceps loading Rotator Cuff Maintenance 3 × 15 reps Maintain shoulder strength — assess for proximal chain contributors Wrist and Forearm Flexibility 3 × 30 sec each Address distal chain tightness — reduces elbow joint stress
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Pain ≤3/10 during isometrics • Morning stiffness <15 min • Load provocation identified and modified • Ready for progressive tendon loading
Phase 2 — Heavy Slow Resistance (Week 4–12)
■ GOALS

■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Tricep extension strength >75% symmetry • Heavy loading tolerated without 24h pain spike • Full elbow ROM pain-free • Ready for plyometric introduction

PAEDIATRIC & SPECIAL
91. Sever's Disease (Calcaneal Apophysitis)
Phase 1 — Load Management & Pain Reduction (Week 0–4)
■ GOALS • Reduce calcaneal apophyseal pain in skeletally immature athlete • Identify and modify provocative sport activities (running, jumping, cleated footwear) • Patient and parent education — benign, self-limiting condition with open growth plate • Heel lift or cushioned orthotics for immediate pain reduction
■ PRECAUTIONS • Sever's disease resolves with skeletal maturity (age 13–15 girls, 14–16 boys) — reassurance essential • Avoid complete rest — maintain modified participation with pain ≤4/10 • Monitor for calcaneal stress fracture in high-volume athletes (exclude with MRI if severe) • Avoid barefoot sport or hard cleated boots on firm surfaces • Growth plate is the site of Achilles insertion — excessive traction causes apophysitis
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Heel Lifts / Cushioned Insoles Wear in all footwear Reduce traction stress at calcaneal apophysis — 12–15 mm heel raise Calf Stretching (gastrocnemius) 3 × 45 sec, twice daily Reduce Achilles traction force on growth plate — standing, knee straight Calf Stretching (soleus) 3 × 45 sec, twice daily Deeper Achilles complex stretching — standing, knee bent Swimming / Cycling 30 min, 3–5×/week Cardiovascular and leg fitness — low calcaneal impact Cryotherapy (post-activity) 10–15 min Reduce local inflammation after training — apply wrapped ice Activity Modification Ongoing Reduce running/jumping volume by 50% — allow modified sport participation
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Pain ≤3/10 with modified activity • Child comfortable walking without limp • Heel squeeze test improving • Ready for progressive strengthening
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
Phase 2 — Strengthening & Graduated Return (Week 4–10)
■ GOALS • Calf and intrinsic foot strengthening to reduce Achilles traction • Graduated return to sport with symptom monitoring • Proprioception and ankle stability • Footwear and orthotics optimisation for long-term management
■ PRECAUTIONS • Apply 24h pain rule — if pain >3/10 next morning, reduce previous day's load • Avoid maximal-effort jumping and sprinting until pain-free at moderate effort • Cleat type matters: moulded rubber better than studded boots on hard pitches
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Bilateral Calf Raises (pain-free range) 3 × 20 reps Begin with both legs — avoid terminal plantarflexion if painful Single-leg Calf Raises (when bilateral tolerated) 3 × 15 reps Progressive Achilles loading — pain-guided Tibialis Anterior / Dorsiflexion Strengthening 3 × 20 reps Balanced lower leg muscle development — reduce calf dominance Short Foot Exercise (arch doming) 3 × 20 reps Intrinsic foot strength — supports calcaneal alignment Single-leg Balance (stable surface) 3 × 30 sec Proprioception and ankle stability — appropriate for paediatric athlete Gradual Return to Running Walk-run program, pain-guided Start on soft surface — introduce sport loads progressively Sport Skills (non-impact initially) Drills, technique work Maintain sport-specific skills without maximal calcaneal impact
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Calf raises pain-free (bilateral and unilateral) • Returning to modified training without next-day flare • Heel squeeze test negative or minimal • Ready for unrestricted sport return
Phase 3 — Full Sport Return & Prevention (Week 10–16)
■ GOALS • Unrestricted sport participation pain-free • Full calf strength and flexibility symmetry

• Long-term self-management skills for patient and family • Recurrence prevention through load awareness
■ PRECAUTIONS • Recurrence common during growth spurts — reassess at each growth phase • Maintain heel cushion in sporting footwear throughout growth period • Educate family: sudden training load increases are primary recurrence trigger
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Full Sport Training (gradual) Progressive return Build to full training volume over 3–4 weeks — monitor weekly Ongoing Calf Stretching Daily, 2 × 45 sec each Lifelong habit during growth years — maintain flexibility Strength Maintenance 2 × per week Calf raises, foot intrinsics — prevent recurrence throughout growth period Load Monitoring Education Ongoing Teach family to recognise early warning signs — morning heel pain on waking

92. Little Leaguer's Shoulder (Proximal Humeral Epiphysiolysis)
Phase 1 — Rest & Physeal Protection (Week 0–8)
■ GOALS • Complete cessation of throwing — mandatory physeal rest period • Allow proximal humeral physis healing and physeal stress reduction • Maintain cardiovascular fitness and non-throwing sport fitness • Patient and parent education on physeal injury and return-to-throw timeline
■ PRECAUTIONS • STRICT: No throwing of any kind for minimum 6–8 weeks — non-negotiable • Physeal stress fracture of proximal humerus — premature return can cause growth disturbance • X-ray comparison views required — physeal widening on affected side is diagnostic • Pitcher's mound restrictions per Little League rules should be discussed with family • Assess pitching mechanics, training volume, and year-round throwing exposure
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Rest from All Throwing Minimum 6–8 weeks Physeal protection — complete throwing cessation mandatory Cardiovascular Fitness (non-throwing) 30 min, 5×/week Bike, swimming, running — maintain general fitness Shoulder Flexibility (gentle AROM) 3 × 20 reps Pain-free shoulder ROM maintenance — no resistive loading Scapular YPSILON Exercises 3 × 15 reps (low load) Scapular stabiliser activation — bodyweight only Core Stabilisation (age-appropriate) 3 × 10 reps Dead bug, bird dog — develop kinetic chain for throwing Lower Limb Strengthening 3 × 15 reps Maintain leg strength — throwing power is lower limb driven
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Pain-free with all daily activities • X-ray evidence of physeal healing (repeat at 6–8 weeks) • No tenderness over proximal humerus • Physician and physiotherapist clearance for progressive loading

■ PRECAUTIONS • No throwing until shoulder strength >80% symmetry • Introduce throwing only under physiotherapist / coach supervision • Avoid maximal effort throwing for 16 weeks minimum • Biomechanical assessment: arm slot, trunk rotation, hip drive are critical
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Rotator Cuff Program (ER/IR, band) 3 × 20 reps, progressive External and internal rotation — physeal stress reduced with careful load Scapular Strengthening (Y, T, W) 3 × 12 reps Restore scapular stability — essential for throwing biomechanics Shoulder ER Strengthening (90/90 position) 3 × 15 reps Sport-specific shoulder position — deceleration strength restoration Thrower's Ten Program (Wilk) 3 × per week Comprehensive rotator cuff and scapular program for throwing athletes Plyometric Ball Work (lightweight) 3 × 10 reps Rhythmic stabilisation — shoulder neuromuscular control Hip and Core Power Development 3 × 12 reps Rotational power from lower limb — reduce arm-dominant throwing pattern
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Full shoulder ROM equal bilaterally (allow 10° ER dominance on throwing side) • Rotator cuff strength ≥80% symmetry • Scapular dyskinesis resolved • Ready for graduated interval throwing program

93. Juvenile Osgood-Schlatter
Phase 1 — Load Management & Symptom Control (Week 0–4)
■ GOALS • Reduce tibial tubercle pain and apophyseal inflammation • Identify and modify patellar tendon traction provocations • Maintain modified sport participation where possible (pain ≤4/10) • Patient and family education — benign, self-limiting with skeletal maturity
■ PRECAUTIONS • Osgood-Schlatter resolves at skeletal maturity when apophysis fuses • Avoid complete rest — deconditioning and return-to-sport complications follow • Monitor for tibial tubercle avulsion fracture in severe cases (rare but surgical emergency) • Hard surfaces and plyometric loading worsen apophyseal traction — modify • Patellar tendon strap (infrapatellar) may provide immediate symptomatic relief
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Patellar Tendon Strap / Infrapatellar Band Wear during all activity Reduce patellar tendon traction on tibial apophysis — immediate pain relief Quadriceps Stretching (prone, standing) 3 × 45 sec each Reduce quadriceps traction force — key conservative intervention Hamstring Stretching 3 × 45 sec each Reduce knee flexion deficit — improve patellar tendon mechanics Swimming / Cycling 30 min, 3–5×/week Maintain cardiovascular fitness — low patellar tendon loading Cryotherapy (post-activity) 10–15 min Reduce local inflammation after activity — ice over tibial tubercle Activity Modification Ongoing Reduce kneeling, jumping, and sprinting volume — not complete rest
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Pain ≤3/10 with walking and modified activity • Tibial tubercle tenderness decreasing • No acute avulsion fracture on clinical assessment • Ready for strengthening program

94. Sinding-Larsen-Johansson Syndrome
Phase 1 — Load Reduction & Protection (Week 0–4)
■ GOALS • Reduce inferior patellar pole pain (patellar cartilage / apophyseal junction) • Differentiate from Osgood-Schlatter (proximal patellar tendon vs. distal) • Activity modification to reduce quadriceps-patellar tendon traction • Reassure patient and family of benign natural history
■ PRECAUTIONS • Inferior patellar pole apophysitis — affects younger athletes (10–13 years) than Osgood-Schlatter • X-ray: irregular calcification at inferior patella pole — normal variant in this age • Avoid deep squat, jumping, and sprinting in high-irritability phase • Patellar tendon strap above patellar pole may reduce traction symptoms • Monitor for patellar sleeve fracture — acute onset with severe swelling warrants urgent X-ray
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Patellar Tendon Strap (superior patellar pole position) During all activity Redirect patellar tendon traction — position strap above tibial tubercle Quadriceps Stretching (prone) 3 × 45 sec, twice daily Primary intervention — reduce quadriceps traction on patellar apophysis Swimming / Cycling 30 min, 3–5×/week Cardiovascular maintenance — low patellar traction load Cryotherapy (post-activity) 10 min Local apophyseal inflammation management — ice inferior pole of patella Activity Modification 50–80% reduction Reduce jumping and stairs — modified participation where possible
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Pain ≤3/10 with walking and modified activity • Patellar pole tenderness reducing • No acute patellar sleeve fracture • Ready for progressive strengthening

95. Perthes Disease (Rehabilitation)
Phase 1 — Acute Phase — Pain Management & Containment (Week 0–12)
■ GOALS • Reduce hip pain and synovitis during avascular necrosis phase • Maintain femoral head containment within acetabulum • Preserve hip ROM — especially abduction and internal rotation • Prevent femoral head collapse and deformity
■ PRECAUTIONS • Multidisciplinary team: orthopaedic surgeon, physiotherapist, occupational therapist required • NWB or restricted weight bearing per orthopaedic protocol — Scottish Rite orthosis may be indicated • Avoid forced hip extension and external rotation in painful phase • Monitor for loss of abduction ROM — early loss predicts worse outcomes • Age of onset predicts prognosis: <6 years: better outcome; >8 years: guarded prognosis
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Pool Therapy / Hydrotherapy 30 min, 5×/week Optimal environment — buoyancy offloads femoral head while maintaining ROM Hip Abduction AROM (supine) 3 × 20 reps Maintain containment in acetabulum — critical range to preserve Hip Internal Rotation AROM 3 × 20 reps Early loss of IR is prognostic indicator — gentle maintenance Hip Flexion / Extension AROM (pain-free) 3 × 20 reps Full ROM maintenance — avoid forced ranges Stationary Bike (low resistance) 20 min Hip ROM through cycling motion — non-weight-bearing if in boot/brace Core Strengthening (supine, pain-free) 3 × 10 reps Maintain lumbopelvic stability — no provocative hip loading
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Hip pain ≤3/10 with daily activities • Hip abduction ROM maintained ≥30° • Orthopaedic clearance for progressive weight bearing • No significant femoral head collapse on imaging
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
Phase 2 — Revascularisation Phase — Strengthening (Month 3–12)
■ GOALS • Progressive hip strengthening as femoral head re-ossifies • Restore hip abductor, flexor, and extensor strength • Normalise gait mechanics — reduce Trendelenburg pattern • Return to modified school activities and low-impact sport
■ PRECAUTIONS • Impact activity restrictions based on Herring classification (A/B/C) and femoral head shape • Contact sports and high-impact activity restricted until orthopaedic clearance • Monitor ROM loss — progressive stiffness indicates disease progression • Limb length discrepancy: assess and correct with heel lift if >1 cm
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Hip Abductor Strengthening (progressive) 3 × 20 reps Side-lying, then band resistance — glute med is critical for Trendelenburg Hip Extensor Strengthening (bridge) 3 × 15 reps Glute max activation — posterior chain support for femoral head containment Hip Flexor Strengthening (pain-free) 3 × 15 reps Seated hip flexion, progressed — maintain iliopsoas strength Swimming (preferred sport) 3–5×/week Non-impact — excellent sport throughout Perthes rehabilitation Gait Retraining Walking sessions Trendelenburg correction — lateral trunk lean feedback and correction Cycling (stationary and outdoor) 30 min, 3×/week Low-impact cardiovascular — appropriate sport for Perthes child
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Hip ROM: abduction ≥40°, flexion ≥120° • Trendelenburg gait resolved or significantly improved • Orthopaedic clearance for return to impact activity • Femoral head shape adequate on X-ray review
Phase 3 — Return to Activity & Long-Term Monitoring (Month 12–24+)
■ GOALS • Return to age-appropriate sport and recreational activities • Full hip strength and ROM symmetry where possible • Long-term joint health education — risk of early hip OA
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
• Lifetime monitoring for residual deformity and hip OA
■ PRECAUTIONS • Long-term risk: Perthes disease predisposes to early hip OA — low-impact sport preferred • Avoid high-impact, high-volume sports (distance running, contact sports) in those with Herring B/C classification • Annual orthopaedic review until skeletal maturity • Identify and refer hip impingement (FAI) — common sequela of Perthes disease
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Low-Impact Sport (swimming, cycling, golf) Age-appropriate Protect remodelled femoral head — maintain fitness without joint overload Hip Strengthening Maintenance 3 × per week Abductors, extensors, external rotators — maintain throughout childhood Flexibility Program Daily Hip flexor, adductor, rotator flexibility — prevent stiffness Age-Appropriate Reintegration Gradual return Return to school sport and physical education — coordinate with orthopaedics
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Full age-appropriate activity within orthopaedic restrictions • Hip strength and ROM optimised for residual deformity • Child and family understand long-term monitoring requirements

96. Peripheral Nerve Injury (Peroneal Nerve Palsy)
Phase 1 — Nerve Protection & Functional Maintenance (Week 0–6)
■ GOALS • Protect recovering peroneal nerve from further compression or traction • Prevent foot drop complications — falls risk management • Maintain passive joint mobility during motor recovery • Patient education on nerve recovery timeline (1 mm/day axonal growth)
■ PRECAUTIONS • Identify and remove causative compression: fibular head pressure, prolonged squatting, cast, cross-legged sitting • Ankle-foot orthosis (AFO) mandatory for foot drop — prevents tripping and equinus contracture • Neurapraxia: recovery 6–8 weeks; axonotmesis: 3–6 months; neurotmesis may require surgical repair • Monitor for pressure sores under AFO — sensory deficit means patient cannot feel skin breakdown • EMG/NCS at 3–4 weeks if no recovery — guides prognosis and surgical planning
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale AFO (Ankle-Foot Orthosis) Provision All weight bearing activities Foot drop correction — prevents equinus and fall risk during nerve recovery Passive Ankle PROM (DF, eversion) 3 × 20 reps each Prevent equinus and peroneal contracture during flaccid phase Tibialis Anterior NMES / FES 20 min, 2×/day if available Electrical stimulation maintains muscle mass during denervation Gait Training (with AFO) Daily sessions Safe ambulation — retrain normal gait pattern with orthotics support Hip and Proximal Strengthening 3 × 15 reps each Maintain hip abductor and extensor strength — compensatory gait reduction Proprioception Training (seated) 3 × 10 reps Maintain sensory input — eyes-open balance work where possible
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Safe ambulation achieved with AFO • No equinus contracture developed • Early motor return signs: trace dorsiflexion or eversion • Causative factor identified and removed
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
Phase 2 — Motor Re-education & Progressive Strengthening (Week 6–16)
■ GOALS • Facilitate motor return in tibialis anterior, peronei, and toe extensors • Progress from trace motor activity to functional strength • Wean from AFO dependency as strength returns • Sensory re-education
■ PRECAUTIONS • Do not remove AFO until tibialis anterior strength ≥3/5 (MRC grading) — falls risk • Avoid fatigue-inducing exercise in denervated muscle — impairs recovery • Monitor for neuroma formation or neuropathic pain • NMES/FES contraindicated if neurotmesis with complete disruption — discuss with neurologist
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Active Dorsiflexion (assisted → active) 3 × 15 reps Neural re-education — gravity-eliminated initially, progress to gravity-resisted Tibialis Anterior NMES-assisted contraction 20 min, 2×/day FES-assisted motor recruitment — combines voluntary effort with electrical Resisted Dorsiflexion (band, when grade 3+) 3 × 15 reps Progressive tibialis anterior loading as motor return allows Peroneal Eversion (band, when grade 3+) 3 × 15 reps Peroneus longus and brevis re-education — functional ankle stability Toe Extension Exercises 3 × 20 reps Extensor digitorum longus and hallucis — functional toe clearance in gait Proprioception Training (standing, progressive) 3 × 30 sec Single-leg balance — progress as dorsiflexion strength returns Sensory Re-education (desensitisation) 10 min, 2×/day Tactile stimulation of lateral leg and dorsum — normalise sensory processing
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Tibialis anterior ≥3/5 MRC strength • Walking without AFO for short distances safely • Sensory return to dorsal foot and lateral lower leg • Ready for functional and sport-specific rehabilitation
Phase 3 — Functional Recovery & Return to Activity (Week 16–52)
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
■ GOALS • Full or near-full motor and sensory recovery • Return to work and sport • AFO weaning completed where nerve recovery permits • Long-term management plan for permanent deficits if recovery incomplete
■ PRECAUTIONS • Complete recovery may take 12 months for axonotmesis • Permanent deficit possible in neurotmesis — tendon transfer surgery may be indicated • Ongoing AFO use required if tibialis anterior <4/5 strength permanently • Surgical referral if no clinical recovery by 3 months post-injury
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Progressive Dorsiflexion Resistance 4 × 15 reps, progressive load Strength symmetry goal — use graded resistance band or weight boot Functional Gait Training (without AFO) Progressive distances Normal heel-strike gait retraining — progress distance and terrain Stair and Ramp Training Daily practice Functional mobility requirements — prevent persistent foot drop adaptation Running Return (when DF ≥4/5) Graduated program Running only when safe foot clearance — begin walk-jog with monitoring Sport-Specific Return Progressive sessions Return to sport demands — continue AFO if any residual foot drop Home Exercise Program (long-term) Daily Dorsiflexion and eversion strengthening — nerve recovery ongoing for 12 months
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Tibialis anterior ≥4/5 MRC (ideally 5/5) • Safe walking and running without AFO • Return to functional work and sport demands

97. Complex Regional Pain Syndrome (CRPS Type I)
Phase 1 — Pain Desensitisation & Gentle Activation (Week 0–8)
■ GOALS • Desensitise hypersensitive peripheral and central nervous system • Reduce allodynia and hyperalgesia through graded exposure • Prevent disuse atrophy and joint contracture • Multidisciplinary team engagement: pain medicine, psychology, physiotherapy
■ PRECAUTIONS • CRPS is a sensitised nervous system disorder — aggressive exercise WORSENS outcomes • Pain is NOT a reliable guide to tissue damage — graded exposure despite pain is required • Psychological assessment mandatory: catastrophising, kinesiophobia, depression are key drivers • Sympathetically maintained pain: sympathetic blockade / SCS referral if conservative fails 3 months • Budapest Criteria required for diagnosis — exclude other pathology • Forced passive mobilisation is contraindicated and can worsen CRPS
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Graded Motor Imagery — Limb Laterality Recognition 10 min, 2×/day Normalise cortical body schema — Moseley program: identify left/right images Graded Motor Imagery — Imagined Movements 10 min, 2×/day Stage 2 GMI — mentally rehearse pain-free movement without physical movement Mirror Therapy 10–20 min, daily Cortical reorganisation — watch reflected unaffected limb performing movements Desensitisation (graded textures) 10 min, 2×/day Cotton wool → washcloth → towel → brush — progressive tactile stimulation Pain-free AROM (minimal effort) 3 × 5 reps, gentle Maintain joint mobility — only movements that do NOT increase symptoms beyond 2/10 Graded Activity (pacing) Daily structured Activity pacing program — set time-based goals, not pain-based goals
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Allodynia reducing — touch tolerance improving • GMI stages 1 and 2 tolerated
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
• Engagement with psychological support • Ready for mirror therapy and movement exposure progression
Phase 2 — Progressive Movement Exposure (Week 8–20)
■ GOALS • Graduated physical activity exposure using pain neurophysiology education • Reduce fear-avoidance behaviours • Restore functional active ROM • Introduce graded weight bearing and functional tasks
■ PRECAUTIONS • Set a maximum tolerable 'pain budget' with patient — typically 3–4/10 during exercise • Flare-ups are common and expected — they do not indicate harm; manage expectation • Avoid overactivation — boom-bust cycles worsen CRPS trajectory • Aquatic physiotherapy: excellent medium — buoyancy, warmth, and tactile input simultaneously
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Pain Neurophysiology Education (PNE) Weekly sessions Explain central sensitisation mechanisms — reduce catastrophising and fear-avoidance Graded Weight Bearing (aquatic) 20 min, 3×/week Pool environment — warmth reduces sympathetic output; buoyancy reduces load Progressive AROM (all planes) 3 × 10 reps, graded Mirror therapy → actual movement — progressive exposure to movement Isometric Strengthening (low effort) 3 × 10 reps, 5 sec Maintain muscle mass — low-grade isometrics below symptom threshold Aerobic Exercise (whole body) 20 min, 3×/week Walking, cycling — systemic endogenous opioid and noradrenergic activation Proprioception Training (seated) 3 × 30 sec Graded balance challenges — normalise sensory processing from affected limb Functional Task Practice Daily structured Occupation-based activity — meaningful tasks improve central sensitisation
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Functional ROM restoration (≥75% of contralateral) • Walking 20 min pain ≤4/10 • Return to basic ADLs independently • Psychological comorbidities managed
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab
physionutrition.vercel.app | Protocols 86–100
Phase 3 — Functional Restoration & Self-Management (Week 20+)
■ GOALS • Return to work, sport, and meaningful activities • Independence in self-management program • Long-term pain management strategies established • Recurrence prevention and relapse management plan
■ PRECAUTIONS • CRPS can have prolonged course — 12–24 months is not unusual • Return to work / sport graded and supported — avoid full sudden return • Recurrence risk: new injury or immobilisation can trigger CRPS in sensitised individuals • Ongoing psychological support and pain management team review
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Progressive Strengthening Program 3 × 15 reps, progressive load Graduated resistance — match pre-CRPS capacity as goal Return to Work / Sport Program Graduated exposure Graded return with occupational therapist or sports physiotherapist Aerobic Exercise (progressive) 30–45 min, 4–5×/week Lifestyle aerobic exercise — most potent endogenous pain modulator Self-Management Education Ongoing Pacing, stress management, sleep hygiene, activity monitoring Relapse Prevention Plan Documented plan Identify early warning signs, early intervention strategies — prevent recurrence
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Return to functional work and recreational activities • Pain ≤3/10 with all daily demands • Independent self-management program in place • Psychological wellbeing maintained

98. Brachial Plexus Neuropraxia (Burner/Stinger)
Phase 1 — Acute Management & Symptom Resolution (Day 0–Week 2)
■ GOALS • Confirm diagnosis and exclude cervical spine injury • Resolve burning/stinging pain and upper limb paraesthesia • Restore full cervical ROM • Same-day return to play assessment (if symptoms resolve fully within minutes)
■ PRECAUTIONS • URGENT: Bilateral symptoms, neck pain, or symptoms lasting >15 minutes = possible cervical spine injury — DO NOT RETURN TO PLAY, cervical spine protocol • Recurrent burners with cervical stenosis: MRI mandatory before return to contact sport • Never return athlete to field with ongoing neurological symptoms • Helmet and shoulder pad fit assessment — impingement at shoulder pad edge causes brachial plexus stretch • Repeated stingers in same season: full neurological and imaging workup required
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Cervical ROM Assessment Immediate sideline Full pain-free ROM in all planes required before return to play consideration Upper Limb Neurological Assessment Immediate sideline Motor: deltoid, biceps, grip; Sensory: dermatome mapping; Reflex: biceps, brachioradialis Cervical Retraction (chin tucks) 3 × 15 reps, gentle Offload neural foramen — gentle traction on irritated upper trunk C5-C6 Shoulder AROM (pain-free) 3 × 20 reps Maintain shoulder mobility — no resistance during acute phase Ice / Cryotherapy 15 min as needed Reduce cervical muscle guarding and acute pain Rest from Contact (if symptoms >15 min) Until resolved Protect recovering neural structures — modified training only
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Full upper limb neurological function restored • Full pain-free cervical ROM in all planes • No bilateral or lower limb symptoms
PhysioNutrition — MSK Rehab Protocols | physionutrition.vercel.app | Evidence-Based Rehab

• Cervical spine injury excluded clinically
Phase 2 — Cervical & Shoulder Strengthening (Week 2–6)
■ GOALS • Restore full cervical and shoulder strength • Identify and correct biomechanical risk factors for recurrence • Neck strengthening to reduce stinger mechanism • Return to full contact training
■ PRECAUTIONS • Cervical MRI if: ≥3 stingers in season, symptoms >24h, or progressive motor deficit • Cervical spinal stenosis (Torg ratio <0.8) is significant contraindication to contact sport return • Shoulder pad fitting review with team equipment manager — critical prevention intervention • Tackle and blocking technique modification — raise shoulder pad profile
Exercise Parameters Clinical Cue / Rationale Deep Cervical Flexor Training 3 × 10 reps, 10 sec hold Longus colli and capitis — primary cervical stabiliser for contact sport Cervical Strengthening (flexion, extension, lateral flexion) 3 × 15 reps, resistance band Progressive resistance — target 30% bodyweight cervical strength Shoulder Shrug and Elevation Strengthening 3 × 20 reps Upper trapezius and levator scapulae — brace effect against brachial plexus stretch Rotator Cuff Strengthening (full program) 3 × 15 reps each Restore full shoulder strength — Thrower's Ten or equivalent Scapular Stabilisation (Y, T, W) 3 × 12 reps Scapular position control — reduce brachial plexus tension with protraction Cervical Proprioception Training 3 × 10 reps Joint position sense retraining — laser or head pointer exercises
■ CRITERIA TO PROGRESS TO NEXT PHASE • Full cervical ROM and strength • Upper limb motor and sensory symmetry • Cleared by physician or team physician for contact • Ready for return to full contact training
